man can do with God! It is of course true, is bound to be, 
that he who resigns his will wholly to God will catch God and 
bind him so that he can do nothing but what that man wills. 
He who makes over his will to God, to him God gives his will 
in return, so wholly, so really that God’s will becomes that 
man’s own, he having sworn by himself to do nothing but 
what that man will, but God will never be anyone’s own who 
has not become his own first. 

St Augustine cries, ‘ Lord thou wilt be no man’s own until 
he has become thine own.’ Night and day we cry to God, ‘ Lord 
thy will be done.’ And when God’s will does come to pass we 
grumble that it is all wrong. If our will is God’s will, well and 
good, but if God’s will is ours that is far better. Suppose thy 
will is God’s will then if thou-art ill thou wilt not, against God’s 

SERMONS 115 

will, desire to be well, but thou wilt wish it were God’s will that 
thou shouldst be well. And when things go wrong with thee 
thou wilt wish it were God’s will that they should go right. 
But when God’s will is thy will then if thou art sick : ‘In God’s 
name’; if thy friend dies: ‘In God’s name.’ It is doubtless 
true and is bound to be that though it should entail all the 
pains of hell, of purgatory and the world, the will in union 
with the will of God would bear all this eternally, for ever in 
hell torment, and take it for its eternal bliss; and resigning, 
in God’s will our Lady’s happiness and her perfection together 
with that of all the saints, it would remain for ever in eternal 
pain and bitterness, not wavering for an instant and with no 
thought of wishing things were otherwise. When the will is 
united so that it forms a single one, then the heavenly Father 
begets his only Son in himself and in me.—Why in himself, in 
me ?—Because I am then one with him, he cannot shut me 
out, and in that act the Holy Ghost receives his being, his 
working, his becoming from me as from God.—Why ?—Because 
I am in God: he cannot exclude me, not in any way. 

Moses’ will had grown to be so wholly God’s will that God’s 
honour with the people was more precious to him than his own 
felicity. God held out promises to Moses which Moses brushed 
aside ; had he promised his whole Godhood Moses would not 
have consented. And Moses besought God, saying: ‘ Lord strike 
me out of the Book of Life.’ Theologians ask, did Moses love 
the people better than himself? They say, no. For Moses 
knew quite well that by fostering reverence for God among the 
people he came nearer God than by being careless of God’s 
honour and seeking his own happiness. And likewise it be- 
hoves the righteous man not to seek his own in all he does, but 
God’s glory only. While in all thy doings thou art turned 
more towards thyself, or towards one person rather than 
another, thy will has not become God’s will. 

Our Lord says in the Gospel, ‘ My teaching is not mine but 
his who sent me.’ And so with the good man it ought to be: 
my work is not my work; my life is not my life. In this 

116 MEISTER ECKHART 

disposition all the perfection, all the bliss St Peter had and 
that St Paul racked his brains to win, with all the felicity they 
have gotten from it, this I enjoy as well as they, and I look to 
enjoy it eternally as though it had been my own doing. More: 
all the work of all the saints and all the angels and that Mary 
God’s mother ever did, from this I hope to reap eternal joy as 
though I had done it all myself. 

I say, man and manhood are different. Manhood in itself 
is a most noble thing, at its highest it is level with the angels 
and near akin to Deity. The straitest union, which Christ had 
with the Father, this it is possible for me to win if only I were 
able to slough off what there is of this and that and realize my 
manhood. All God ever gave his only Son he has given me 
as much as him, no less. He has given me more. He gave 
more of my humanity in Christ to me than him, for he gave 
him nothing : he has had it in the Father eternally. Supposing 
I slay thee, I slay in the first place a Richard or a Henry, after- 
wards the man. Not so God. He took on manhood first. 
Who is man ? He who is named after Jesus Christ’s manhood. 
Hence our Lord says in the Gospel, ‘ Whoso offends one of these 
is offending me.’ 

But to return. * Moses besought the Lord his God.’ People 
are always asking God to do his best for them, but they have 
no intention of giving their best to God ; they want to share 
with God, giving him a little and the worst at that. But the 
first thing God gives is himself. And having God thou hast 
all things with God. I have sometimes said, he who has God 
and all things with God has no more than one who has God 
alone. Also I maintain that a thousand angels are in eternity 
no more than two or one, because there is no number in eternity, 
it transcends number. 

“Moses besought the Lord his God.’ Moses, that means one 
who is rescued from the water. But it is the will I want to 
speak of now. To give an-hundred marks of gold for God is 
a noble deed, and as such does it appear, yet I declare that if 
I have the will but no hundred marks to give, then, granting 

SERMONS 117 

the genuine intention, I have paid God the gold and he is bound 
to answer me as though I had given him the hundred marks. 
More, I say, had I a whole world to give, then with that in- 
tention I have given that whole world to God and he must 
answer me as though I had given a whole world to him. Isay, 
if I had slain the pope with my own hand but unintentionally, 
I would none the less go up to the altar and say mass as usual. 
I say, manhood in the wretchedest, most despicable man is as 
perfect asit isin king or pope, and manhood in itself I hold more 
dear than the man in me. May we be thus atoned with God, 
So help us the truth whereof I speak. Amen. 

XI 

Beati qui esuriunt et sitiunt justiciam etc. (Matth. 5,). And 
Qui sequetur justiciam diligetur a domino (Prov. 15,). I have 
taken one sentence from the epistle for the day and another 
from the gospel. King Solomon says in the epistle, ‘ God loves 
them that follow after righteousness.’ St Matthew has another 
saying: ‘ Blessed are the poor and they that hunger and thirst 
after righteousness, that follow after it, (for they shall be 
filled)” Mark the words, ‘ God loves.’ As I have often said, 
a great reward is mine, no less than being loved by God, always 
providing I desire it. What does God love? God loves 
nothing but himself and his own likes so far as he can find them 
in me and in him. In the Book of Wisdom it is written, ‘ God 
loves none but the man who abideth in wisdom.’ And there 
is another saying, still better, in the scriptures: ‘ God loves 
those who follow after justice and wisdom.’ Theologians are 
agreed that God’s wisdom is his only Son. It says in this 
passage, ‘those who follow after righteousness and wisdom,’ 
so he loves those who follow him : he loves nothing in us except 
in so far as he finds us in him. ‘hey are worlds apart, God’s 
loving and our loving. We love only inasmuch as we find God 
in what we love. Had I forsworn it yet could I love nothing 
but goodness. But God loves a man inasmuch as he is good, 
not that he finds in man anything to love excepting his own 

118 MEISTER ECKHART 

goodness, and so far as we are in him and in his love, that is 
given. It is thanks to his love that we are in him and dwell- 
ing in wisdom. 

According to St Paul, ‘ we shall be changed in love.’ Mark 
the words, ‘God loves.” A miracle! What is God’s love ? 
His nature and his essence, that is his love. To rob God of his 
love for us would rob him of his being, of his Godhood, seeing 
that his love for me is his very self. And in this wise the Holy 
Ghost proceeds. Good God, what miracle is this! If God is 
loving me with his whole nature, and this depends upon it, 
then God loves me just as though his being and becoming hung 
thereon. God has but one love: the selfsame love with which 
the Father loves his Son, therewith he loves me. 

Now another meaning. At the same time you will see how 
true the scriptures are if one takes the trouble to find out what 
they mean. He says, ‘those who follow after righteousness in 
wisdom.’ To the just man justice is so necessary that he can 
love nothing but justice. As I often say, were God not just 
he would care nothing for God: Wisdom and Justice are the 
same in God. There the lover of truth loves justice too, and if 
the devil were just he would love him as well, so far as he was 
just and not a hair’s-breadth more. The just man loves 
nothing of God’s, this or that ; if God were to give him all his 
wisdom and everything he could except himself, he would take 
no pleasure in it or care for it at all: he wants nothing, seeks 
nothing, has no cause for doing anything. As God works 
without a why and has no cause, so does the just man work 
without a why, and as life is living for its own sake and seeks 
no cause for living so neither has the just man any motive for 
his deeds. 

Then as to the words, ‘they hunger and thirst after righteous- 
ness.’ What are we to understand by this? Our Lord says, 
‘ They that eat me shall hunger, they that drink me shall in- 
crease their thirst.’ What does this mean? It is not true 
of bodily things: the more a man eats the more he is filled. 
But with spiritual things there is no satiety, the more we have 

SERMONS 119 

the more we want. Hence the words, ‘ They shall grow thirstier, 
those who drink me, and hungrier those who eat me.’ These 
are so hungry for the will of God and relish it so much, every- 
thing God sends them is so much to their liking, pleases them 
so well, they would not wish it otherwise. While a man is 
hungry he enjoys his food, and the hungrier he is the more 
satisfaction does he find in eating. It is the same with those 
_ who hunger after righteousness and the will of God: to them 
his will, whatever God wills, is very good, they are so satisfied 
with everything he sends that supposing God wanted to spare 
them they would refuse to be spared. So content they are 
with the sovran will of God. IfI wanted to get into somebody’s 
good graces, wanted to please him and him alone, then the 
things that person liked and which gave him pleasure would 
be my first care. Suppose I pleased him better in fustian than 
in velvet then fustian doubtless I should wear in preference to 
any other dress. And the same with anyone who delights to 
do the will of God: whatever God may send, sickness or 
poverty or whatever it may be, he will prefer, since it is God’s 
will. He will like it much better than anything else. 

Here you will be asking, ‘ How do I know it is God’s will ? ’— 
Believe me, were it not God’s will then in a trice it would not 
be. It must always be his will. If now the will of God seemed 
good to thee thou wouldst be as it were in heaven no matter 
what might happen or not happen to thee, and those who desire 
something other than God’s will get also their just due: they 
are always complaining and unhappy, always the victims of 
violence and wrong, constantly in trouble. It is only just. 
They are selling God just as Judas sold him. They love God 
for something else, not God. And they get, quite rightly what, 
forgetting God himself, they love him for—devotion or delight 
or whatever coin they may be paid in. These things are none 
of them God. 

It is written that ‘The world was made by him, and that 
which was made knew him not.’ Anyone who fondly thought 
a thousand worlds plus God was more than God alone would 

120 MEISTER ECKHART 

know nothing of God, he would have no notion what God is 
and would be a boor. Never mind what is with God. As I 
have often said, he who seeks aught in addition to God knows _ 
not what he seeks. 

When the Son is born in us we, being without why, are re- 
born in the Son. Origen has a fine saying which I will quote, 
although it may sound to you incredible: ‘ We are not only 
in-born in the Son, we are born out and born in again and new 
born and born without means in the Son.’ I say, and it is true, 
that in every good thought and good deed and good intention 
we are always born anew in God. And, as I was saying, the 
Father has got nothing but one only Son, so the less we pay 
attention to and care for other things than God, the more we 
stop looking out, the more we shall be transformed in the Son 
and the more the Son will be born in us and we be born in the 
Son and be the Son. Our Lord Jesus Christ is the only Son of 
the Father and he alone is man and God. There there is but one 
Son, one in nature: the divine nature. We shall be made one 
with him if our affections are set on him alone. God is ever. 
alone, that must needs be, and the only thing for us to do is 
to bear God alone in mind. 

God has poured out into creatures joy and satisfaction, true, 
but the root of satisfaction, the essence of enjoyment God has 
reserved to himself alone. Take an illustration. Fire, in its 
heat, throws out its roots into water, and when the fire goes out 
its warmth remains in the water for a while, and so it does 
in wood, according to the time the fire was present and also 
to its heat. The sun, on the other hand, shines through the 
air and illumines it, but its roots it does not throw therein, for 
when the sun is gone we have no more light. It is the same 
with God and creatures. The appearance of satisfaction he 
throws into creatures, hut the root he keeps in him, so that he 
may have us for himself and himself alone. God has put him- 
self out for the soul and has gone half-way to meet her. He 
has tried with all his godhood to make himself agreeable to the 
soul, for God intends to please the soul, and he will brook no 

SERMONS 121 

rival. God permits of no compulsion, but neither will he be 
a party to our loving or desiring aught but him. 

Some people fondly thinking they are very holy, very perfect, 
take to talking big and having large ideas; they are pompous 
and pretentious and make much ado about themselves, about 
this thing and that, pretending to devotion although they are 
impatient of a word. You may be sure that they are far from 
God and not in union with him. The prophet says,.‘ I have 
poured out my soul into myself,’ and St Augustine says better : 
I have poured out my soul over myself: needs must she rise 
above me to become one in the Son; and in so far as she 
transcends herself she does become one with the Son. St Paul 
says, “We shall be transformed into the same image that 
he is.’ 

It has been said that virtue is no virtue except it is from God 
_ or for God or in God. It must be one of these three. Other- 
wise it would not be virtue, for the human will is too feeble 
‘without God. Virtue is God or immediately in God. But 
which is the best I will not tell you now. You will say, ‘ Tell 
us, Sir, what thismeans. How can we be in God with nothing 
* between, not minding, not seeking aught else than God ? How 
‘can we be as poor as that and give up everything ? It is a hard 
saying that we must look for no reward.’—Be sure God will not 
fail to give us all; though he swore not to he could not but 
give us himself. Far greater is his need to give to us than ours 
is to take; but we ought not to look for it, the less we look 
and long for it the more God gives. In this way God designs 
simply to make us richer and more able to receive. 

I am in the habit of saying sometimes when I pray: Lord, 
it is such a little thing we ask, were anyone to ask me I would 
do it for him, and it is not only a hundred times easier for thee 
than me, but also thou art more inclined to do it; and even 
if we asked thee for some greater thing it would become thee 
well to grant it ; and the bigger it is the more gladly dost thou 
give it, for God is ready to give royally, provided we give up 
everything in the way we ought. May we follow after right- 

122 MEISTER ECKHART 

eousness in wisdom, hunger and thirst for it till we get our fiii, 
So help us God. Amen. 

XII! 

Euge serve bone et fidelis (Matt. 25,,). We read in the Gospel 
the saying of our Lord, ‘ Well done good and faithful servant, 
enter thou into the joy of thy lord: because thou hast been 
faithful over little I will set thee over all my goods.’ 2 

Let us examine the words used by our Lord. He says, 
*‘ good and faithful servant.’ Now in another Gospel our Lord 
says to a disciple who addressed him as ‘ good,’ ‘ Why callest 
thou me good? none is good but God only,’ which is of course 
true. Creature as such is not good. Nothing whatever is good 
except God alone. Did God then contradict himself? No, 
not at all. The argument is this. So far as man denies himself 
for God and is atoned with God to that extent he is more 
God than creature. The man who quits himself for God, who 
belongs to none but God and lives for none save God alone, verily 
he is by grace what God is by nature and God knows no difference 
between him and this man. I say, ‘ by grace,’ for God is and 
this man is, and as God is good by nature so this man is good 
by grace, since it is God’s life, his being, entirely, which is in 
this man. Our Lord called this man good—‘ good servant ’ 
are the words he used—for this servant is good in the sight of 
God with no other goodness than that wherewith God is good. 
I have often said that God’s life and God’s being are in a stone, 
in a bit of wood and in other creatures which are not blest. 
God is in this servant in a different way which makes him blest 
and good : he is in him for his own enjoyment, living in and 
with him as blissfully, as consciously as in and by himself, so 
he is blest and good. That is why our Lord says, ‘ good 
servant.’ 

1 See also Schmidt’s No. 11 (marked ‘ Eckhart’ in his index), which 
has been freely used in the translation. For a modern German version, 
see Büttner, vol. i, No. 7. 

? Repetitions of these words have been omitted where they appeared 
to be unnecessary. 

SERMONS 123 

Having said something of his goodness, why this servant was 
good, I will now tell you of his faithfulness, for our Lord says 
“good and faithful servant, thou hast been faithful over little,’ 
etc. Let us see what this ‘little’ is over which the servant 
has been faithful. Everything God has created in heaven and 
on earth, what is not himself, all this counts as little in his 
eyes. Over all these things this good servant has been faithful. 
I will tell you in what way. God has set this servant betwixt 
eternity and time. He was not pledged to either, he was free 
in mind and will and in every way. In his mind he ran through 
all the things God has created ; in his will he gave up every- 
thing including his own self and all God has created, (all) that 
is not God himself. Mentally he took them up, and giving God 
praise and glory for them, surrendered them to God, to his un- 
fathomable nature, and himself to boot so far as he is creature. 
There he left himself and all things never to touch them any 
more, or anything created, with his created will. Verily, any 
one so faithful, God would have in him such ineffable delight 
that to rob him of his joy would be to rob him at one stroke 

- of his life, his being, and his Godhood. Further I say, fear not, 
this joy is near you, is in you: there is not one of you so un- 
enlightened, so dull-witted, so remote, but he may find this 
joy within himself, really, as it is, with joy and with under- 
standing, before ye leave this church to-day, aye, before I finish 
preaching, he may find it all within him, feel it, possess it, as 
true as God is God and Iam aman. Be sure of this, for it is 

- true, the truth himself has said so, as I am going to show you 

in a parable taken from the Gospel. 

Once upon a time our Lord was sitting on a well, for he was 
weary. Then came a woman, a Samaritan and heathen, 
bringing a pitcher and a line, meaning to draw water. Our 
Lord said to her, ‘ Woman, give me to drink.’ She answered 
him, saying, ‘Why dost thou ask drink of me? Thou art of 
the Jews and I am a Samaritan; our people and your people 
have no dealings with each other.’ Our Lord replied, saying, 
* Didst thou know who it is that asks thee for drink, and knewest 

124 MEISTER ECKHART 

the gift of God, -peradventure thou wouldst have asked to drink 
of me and I would have given thee living water. Whosoeveı 
drinketh of this water that I give shall never thirst and from 
it shall spring up a fountain of eternal life.’ The woman was 
struck by our Lord’s words, and going close up to the well, she 
asked, ‘ Sir, give me to drink of this water that I may never 
thirst again.’ Then said our Lord, ‘ Go and bring thy husband.’ 
She said, ‘Sir, I have no husband.’ And our Lord replied, 
‘Woman, thou hast well said, for thou hast had five husbands, 
and him thou now hast is not thine husband.’ At this she 
dropped her jug and line and addressed our Lord, saying : 
‘Who art thou? It is written that when Messias cometh whom 
men call Christ, he shall teach us all things and shall make 
known to us the truth.” Quoth our Lord, ‘ Woman, I am he, 
who is talking to thee,’ and at these words her heart was full. 
* Lord,’ she said, ‘ our fathers worshipped under the trees and 
on the mountain and your fathers the Jews worshipped in the 
Temple. Sir, to which prayer does God hearken most and 
which is the best place? Tell me.’ Then said our Lord, 
“Woman, the day cometh and now is when true worshippers 
shall worship not only on the mountain and in the Temple but 
in [the Holy] spirit and in truth; for God is spirit, and they 
that worship him must worship him in spirit and in truth, aid 
the Father seeketh such to worship him.’ At this the woman 
was filled with God, filled to overflowing, welling over with 
divine fullness, and crying with a loud voice, wanting to bring 
to God everyone she saw and make them full of God like her- 
self. This befell, mark you, when she had fetched her husband. 
God never gives himself to the soul overtly, wholly, unreservedly, 
unless she brings her man, that is, her free will. 

Our Lrrd said, ‘Woman, thou sayest well, thou hast had five 
husbands, who are dead, and he whom thou now hast is not 
thine.’ - Who were these five husbands? They were her five 
senses. Therewith she had sinned and therefore they were 
dead. * And the husband thou now hast is not thine,’ meaning 
her free will, which did not belong to her, for it was bound in 

SERMONS 125 

mortal sin’and was not under her control, so it was not hers, 
for what a man does not control does not belong to him, rather 
is it his who can control it. I say that when a man, in grace, 
has mastered his free will and is ready to unite it with the will 
of God, wholly and as a single one, then he need only say, as 
this woman did, Lord, tell me how to pray and what to do to 
please thee best. And Jesus answers, reveals himself that is to 
say, really and wholly and all, all he is, filling the soul to over- 
flowing ; it comes welling up and running over with the over- 
full fullness of God, as it quickly happened with this woman 
at the well who before was quite unready for it. And so I say 
again, as I have said before, there is no one here so dull, so 
ignorant or so unprepared but if by grace he were to atone 
his will wholly, perfectly with the will of God, then he would 
only need to say with desire and intention, ‘Lord, show me 
thy will and strengthen me to do it,’ and God does so, as surely 
as he lives. God will give him fullness as bountiful and perfect 
as he ever gave that woman. So, you see, the most benighted 
of you, the most insignificant among you may have gotten this 
from God before he leaves this church to-day—aye, while I am 
still speaking—really, as true as God is God and I am a man. 
Wherefore I say, fear not, this joy is not far from you, if only 
ye will seek it with a will. 

Now to return. Our Lord said, ‘I will set thee over all 
my goods.’ What is meant by the Lord’s goods? It means 
goodness as scattered and covered up in all things and all 
creatures which are good with his goodness in heaven and on 
earth. That is what is meant by the Lord’s goods. For none 
is good, nor has goods or goodness save from him only. It is 
all his property, and so is everything that we can say of God 
himself or can grasp with the mind or in any way bring to 
light and prove. All this belongs to the Lord, and overit all 
will be set this servant who has been good and faithful over 
little: over all this good he is (now) lord, another and yet the 
same, a something that is but is not this or that and is neither 
here nor there. 

126 MEISTER ECKHART 

Now I have told you what the Lord’s good means. He said, 
“I will set thee over my whole good,’ as though to say, ‘ Have 
done with all created good, all separate good, all piecemeal 
good, over all this will I set thee in the uncreated, undivided, 
undismembered good that I am myself. Referring to this he 
says, ‘Enter thou into the joy of thy Lord,’ or, in other words, 
give up all joy which is partial, which is not of itself that which 
it is, and seek the impartible joy which of itself and in itself is 
that which it is and which is none other than the joy of the 
Lord. 

One word more. What is the joy of the Lord? A curious 
expression. How should one ‘explain or speak, even, of what 
no man can know or understand? But mark this about it. 
The joy of the Lord is the Lord himself, none other, and the 
Lord is living, essential, actual intellect which understands 
itself and is and is living itself in itself and is the same. To 
it I attribute no mode; I have taken from it all mode, for it is 
itself modeless mode which is and is glad that it is. This is 
the joy of the Lord and is the Lord himself. Into this he bade 
his servant enter, saying, ‘Good ‘and faithful servant enter 
thou into the joy of thy lord. Because thou hast been faithful 
(over little) therefore will I set thee over my whole good.’ 
May we too be good and faithful, so that our Lord’shall invite 
us also to enter in and dwell with him eternally, So help us 
God. Amen, 

XIV? 

Christ said, ‘ Blessed are they that hear the word of God 
and keep it.” Let us see what this means. The Father him- 
self hears nothing, knows nothing, says nothing, gets nothing 
but this same Word, and in this same Word the Father hears 
and knows and begets himself as well as this same Word and 
all things and his Godhood fundamentally and himself as to 
his nature and this Word with this same nature in another 
Person. Then as to the manner of his speaking. The Father. 

1 Excerpt, See also Schmidt, No. 40. 

SERMONS 127 

bearing fruit, delivers himself of his own nature all at once in 
his eternal Word, intellectually not willingly, as an act of will. 
Things done or spoken by the power of his will in that same 
power he can also not do an he will. It is not so with the 
Father and his eternal Word: whether he would or no he 
must speak this Word, begetting it without ceasing: with the 
Father it is as it were rooted in his nature naturally just as 
the Father is himself. So you see the Father speaks the Word 
- willingly but not by will, naturally not by nature. In this 
Word the Father speaks my and thy and each man’s spirit 
equally with that same Word. In that speaking thou and I 
are the natural Son of God just the same as the Word itself. 
For, as I have said, the Father knows nothing but this Word 
and himself and all divine nature and all things in this Word, 
and everything he knows therein tallies with the Word and by 
nature is in truth the Word itself. 

When the Father gives, reveals to thee this gnosis, he is giving 
thee his life and his being and his Godhood all at once and really 
and in truth. The mortal father shares with his child his 
nature, not his being or his life. The child has another life 
and being than that which the father has himself. The proof is 
this. The father may die and the child live, or the child may 
die and the father live. With the same life and being they 
must needs live and die together, since the life and being of 
them both would then be one. It is not so with the mortal 
father and his son. In this they are strangers to each other, 
separated as they are from one another in life and being. If 
I take fire from one place to another it makes separate fires : 
this may burn and that go out or this go out and that one go on 
burning. It is neither one nor yet eternal. 

But as I was saying: the Father of heaven gives thee his 
eternal Word, and in that Word he is giving thee at once his 
very life, his being and his Godhood ; for the Father and the 
Word are two Persons and one life and one being undivided. 
“ When the Father finds thee in this light beholding intellectually 
this light—according to its property—in this light itself even 

128 MEISTER ECKHART 

as he in his paternal power perceives himself and all things as 
the Word in word and truth, then he gives thee the power of 
begetting, with himself, thyself and all things and his own _ 
power even this same Word. So thou with the Father and in 
the Father’s power art begetting thyself and all things without 
ceasing in the present now. In this light, as I have said, the 
Father knows no difference between thee and him nor any- 
thing to choose, whether more or less, between him and his 
Word. Forthe Father and the soul and allthings and the Word 
itself are one in that light. 

XV 

‘Master, where dwellest thou ?’ said a certain man to our 
Lord. Christ answered and said unto him, ‘ Come and see.’ 
These words tell us of three things: of the abundant wisdom 
of Christ’s teaching; of his infinite being which is the basis 
of all being—(these two) by the question, ‘Where dwellest 
thou ? ’—and thirdly of God’s comforting invitation to us to 
seek him in spirit in his divine abode and learn for ourselves 
in the school of wisdom. ‘Come,’ he says, ‘and stay with 
me and in me. To thee will I open the abyss of my heart.’ 
Another time our spirit is snatched out of the presence of God. 
Know, all creatures are asking this question, they are eager 
for being, to find divine being ; all study of the works of nature 
is nothing but a search, a quest for, a question about the 
dwelling of God. Unless they did this heaven and the firma- 
ment would be at a standstill. But, my children, why ask 
outside yourselves and seek for God in strange lands of dead 
things ? Not there shall ye find him. They all say nay and 
send you on, crying, ‘We are not God.’ St Augustine’s 
counsel is, ‘ Lift thyself up above thyself, out into eternal 
things." God is there.’ Now you must understand that man 
finds God in many ways; from these the soul may take a 
lesson. 

To begin with, God is found in the exaltation of at-one-ment, 

SERMONS 129 

when the soul is trying with all her might to give up her own 
will. The more the soul makes a practice of this the more apt 
she is to find God in her and herself in him. This is what the 
bridegroom means where he says, in the Book of Love, ‘I will 
go to the mountain of myrrh and will speak with my friend.’ 
The mountain of bitter myrrh is the high and noble frame of 
mind which transmutes all desire for personal satisfaction into 
bitterness towards everything God would not have in us. Then 
God says in spirit to the soul, ‘ Friend mine, how beautiful 
thou art: thou art spotless in mine eyes.’ But he who floats 
with the tide of his own will does not find God, and he is un- 
successful in all his undertakings. 

Secondly, God is found in the bush which grows in the 
desert, where Moses found him. The bush in the desert is the 
higher mind which, in a land quite strange to creatures, is 
growing green and flowering on the heights of the eternal 
Godhead. And just as divine being enters into, is embraced 
in the three Persons, so this mind embraces God in its triple 
powers. This growing causes the soul to wax in light and in 
love till she sees God in Zion in the aspect of an angel. Inas- 
much as thou hast found God thou wilt turn from what is 
unlike him, and to-day more than yesterday. But in order 
to find God like this a man has to cast out all animal feelings 
and with Moses obey the law of the mind, for flesh and blood 
cannot possess the kingdom of God. I ween, O soul, that 
all thy shortcomings are due to the fact that sudden emotions 
affecting the heart from without are translated by thee into 
action ere the light of discrimination shines in. 

Thirdly, God is found on the mountain in the cloud where the 
eternal law of God was written with God’s finger. The 
mountain is the pregnant and great-hearted soul who finds no 
peace or satisfaction in his work unless he, like St. Paul, has 
gotten a definite pledge, in whatever he does, of its being 
God’s will, for then the will of the soul makes for ends not only 
human and proper to itself but divine as well, according to 
some prescribed form of God’s will, so that the soul can say 

130 - MEISTER ECKHART 

with St Paul, ‘I no longer live, but Christ liveth in me. I 
neither work nor do I Jive any more; it is the actual power 
of the divine essence which is energizing in me.’ This takes 
place in the cloud, in the dazzling splendour of the divine — 
light, for any light that man may get from creatures is as 
night to day. Though it may be a help in finding the divine 
light yet creatures are a hindrance if we linger upon them too 
long with delight. Says one of the saints, ‘ The creatures God 
has given us for a step-ladder to God we have ourselves made 
into an offence by settling down in them and stopping on our 
way to safety.’ 

Then, God is found in the cave by the prophet. We read 
that the’ prophet went into the desert and wished for his 
soul that she might die, for his spirit had grown weary 
of the restlessness of this world. And in his sleep an angel 
came and set beside him a hearth-cake and a cruse of water. 
After that he journeyed forty days and nights till he came to 
the place where he found God. There arose a tempest, break- 
ing the rocks in pieces; but God was not in the wind, for God 
shuns the spirits that are driven by the wind, like those the 
prophet Daniel saw struggling in the sea: in lawless hearts 
irrational hopes and fears which blind the light of the spirit. 
And the tempest also means to us the restless heart which is 
ever a bitter trial to his fellow Christians. Breaking the rocks 
means disturbing the peace of serious-minded people. After- 
wards came a fire, and God was not inthe fire. Fire is a thing 
that never cries ‘enough.’ It stands for the heart that never 
cries ‘enough,’ which has an inordinate burning desire for 
things that are not godly. Such ingratitude dries up the flow 
of the divine river. This was followed by a sweet May dew. 
God came in that. It refers to the mind in which, in divine 
imitation of the sweet uses in the eternal Word, there is formed. 
the word of the intellect wherein the yearning soul softly 
whispers with God, wordlessly, noiselessly, singing her minne- 
song in him but without sound. God comes in this, for in 
intimate converse of this sort God is surely drawn into the soul. 

SERMONS 131 

Of this surety they are unworthy who too freely admit from 
without the words of men or even of an angel. Hence the 
desire of the bride where, in the Book of Beautiful Love, she 
invites the north wind to sweep through her: (to sweep out), 
she means, all the lumber of creatures blocking the way of the 
spirit. ‘Towards this same north wind was turned the seething 
caldron of the prophet’s vision, wherein the freshness of God’s 
gift was all withered up, and his soul was disquieted within 
him, for he had no abiding-place in spiritual things. So the 
soul cries, ‘Come, sweet south wind, blow through my garden 
and make my heart flow with divine fragrance.’ 

In the fourth place, God is found above the angels, for the 
soul must transcend the angelic light if she wants to find God, 
although she is by nature lower than the angels. Next, God 
is found in the Father. Thy soul must offer up all she does 
to God, quite free from thine own selfhood, if thou wouldst 
find him, just as the eternal Word offers himself up in the 
Father. 

Sixthly, John found God. ‘In the beginning was the Word,’ 
he says. Andrew and with him every faithful soul inquires, 
‘Master, where dwellest thou?’ John tells you, in the begin- 
ning. Let us seek God so that we find him in himself, so help 
us God. Amen. 

XVI 

In the hands let the lanterns burn. Dionysius says God has 
joined the end of the first to the end of the next. The first 
creature is simply intelligent, an angel. The second one, man, 
is intelligent and at the same time matter. What time the 
light suffuses the soul’s darkness there kindles the first light 
of the soul, the discriminating power which seeks the truth in 
shifting images which wax and wane. In blissful peace, free 
from motion, there alone does the light of pure intellect burn 
with insight into the truth and hence one doctor (speaks of) 
‘the light of wisdom sparkling in the shadow of the rational 
mind.’ As upon astep-ladder the soul mounts up to knowledge 

132 MEISTER ECKHART 

of the first nature. In a certain means the soul is conveyed 
into the First Cause. But eternal heavenly things both 
corporal and mental can unite with the lowest in this light 
only in so far as there reigns in the soul the first work of God 
which is prior to and the basis of all God’s revelations to the 
soul; and this theologians call a light, grace namely. And 
seeing we cannot be happy without it therefore God bids us 
carry this light in our hands, not smother it in ourselves, and 
in that same light climb back to God, where we possess our 
highest happiness. 

In these words God teaches us two things, which concern 
the welfare of all serious people, namely, to know and to love. 
The first is proper to the Son who in his radiant emanated image 
leads the soul into the bottomless abyss of the overflowing 
intellect of the Father. The second is proper to the Holy Ghost 
who in the exuberance of his bliss sweeps the soul up and over 
all things and makes hard and bitter the service of creatures 
by reason of the claims of a higher love. The first stands for 
God in the light, the second for God in the fire. 

Concerning the first the scriptures speak of seven kinds of 
light. These are spiritual activities, activities, I mean, in 
which the spark of true intelligence shines and unceasingly 
orders the work to the highest good of the soul as to its end. 
Not with any creaturely object in view, for thereby all gain to 
the soulislost. This requires that in all thy doings thou observe 
the law of truth which is shining eternally in the highest 
kingdom of thy soul. It is the ray or spark of the soul which 
is giving us counsel all the while, so that thou shalt pass on to 
any given person what is an open book to you concerning him, 
as though all human nature were contained in thee and thy 
nature were everybody’s nature, thou seeing thyself in every- 
one and everyone in thee. This gives an extraordinary beauty 
and delight to the operations of the soul, a beauty not discerned 
by fleshly eyes. It is recorded by a brother that often while 
engaged in prayer he was in a state of such unspeakable sweet- 
ness that he would not have wished his peace of mind disturbed 

SERMONS 133 

were it to see at his cell door Christ in his perfect manhood as 
he was born of’ Mary’s womb, yet in the mortal face of Christ 
the angels take ineffable delight. My children, conform your 
works to this pattern in the secret places of your mind and 
purify your desires in accordance with the sovran good which 
is shining in the soul. When thy mind is freed from its 
activities and wholly abstracted into the unconditioned divine 
state then all fear and narrow-mindedness must die in thee, 
no matter what is thrust in from outside. Then it is given to 
thy spirit to be guided in all it does by the spirit of God. That 
sometimes, owing to the divine abstraction of the soul from 
mortal affairs, the body, returning to its nature, lingers in the 
midst of pleasure, is due to the influx of divine light into all 
activities. 

The second light is the light of faith, a form of light as it were 
natural to the soul. The soul is not found in the light above 
this except by reflection. The light of faith takes the soul on 
into God, though only an image as in a mirror, not naked 
essence (?), which we in this world are not able to see unveiled. 
But on the contrary some doctors say God might create a 
creature to whom he gave a natural light brighter than the 
light of. glory wherein the angels are beholding face to face. 
‘Why should not God,’ they ask, ‘ throw a light into the soul 
in which she could see God undisguised ?’ I answer, were God 
to give the soul a natural light a thousand times as bright 
as the light of glory she could not see God in it; for this 
vision is gained in an ineffable supernatural touching, a 
sinking immediately into the soul of the essential divine 
form. This heavenly informing cannot happen to thee here 
direct ; but. during thy sojourn here thou canst at least turn 
inwards, always keeping thy mind free from sudden agita- 
tions, which hinder clear vision of the divine essence. When 
this (beatific vision) does befall in thee thou shalt discover 
that everything under the sun is vanity and vexation of 
spirit. 

134 MEISTER ECKHART 

XVII 
THE BEATIFIC VISION 

‘Behold I make all things new.’ St John says this in the 
Book of Revelation, and we read it at the annual church festival. 
Augustine says it is good to seek the will of God, and asks ‘ why 
does God do so and so?’ But.to seek the cause of the will of 
God is to seek something prior to, higher than God, whose will 
is himself. He who seeks (n)aught shall find naught. Beware 
then of human curiosity, lest seeking what is naught we lose 
the aught which (is) eternal. Although now we see no cause 
why God created creatures, yet we have some indication in 
the fact that creatures, various angels and some men, have 
exuberant delight in the three Persons, in their eternal inter- 
course and uses, in the intellectual vision of God’s essential 
nature, in the undefined real presence of the formless form of 
divine Being. In the highest light of mental gnosis, therein 
the ground of beatitude lies. Accordingly there springs up 
and grows within the will a prelude of delights which: the 
vision adorns as bloom does youth, and therein is revealed to 
all creatures the splendour and majesty of his estate, and 
concerning it he says, ‘ Behold I make all things new.’ Our 
annual festival commemorates the eternal happening depicted 
here. But since God has specially designed his joys for the 
human soul, that she may be happy in him, therefore the 
words apply to us in two particulars. First, to the noble 
nature of the soul, for he calls the soul by the name of all 
things. The second reference is to the natural end (perfection) 
of the soul, which consists in bringing the soul, by knowing 
and loving, face to face with unveiled divine nature. And 
this he does for us in the renewing of the soul. 

Now mark in the first place how the soul is all things. She 
has being with the stones and growing with the trees and feeling 
with the beasts and understanding with the angels. Moreover, 
she is just the same apart from form and intellect. According 
to the author of the book On the First Cause the soul is created 

SERMONS 135 

on the confines of eternity and time, so she sees into both and 
contains them both intelligibly. All things lead up to man. 
God made man last of all his creatures, on the sixth day, and 
was pleased thereon to feast his eyes, for in the soul he recog- 
nized his own reflection. Again, we might doctrinally liken 
the soul to all things, for the soul can win the blessings and 
rewards of all deserving creatures as we see especially with 
the soul of Christ and of our Lady, more even than the angels. 
For the soul goes on growing without end. Also, when the 
soul has deserved reward of the rewarding grace of Christ the 
bestowal has been wholly disproportionate, so that no creature 
can of its own nature see true in the mirror of God’s nature, 
not even angels of the highest choir, excepting one soul, Christ’s, 
Wherefore our Lord says, ‘ And I if I am lifted up from earth 
will draw all things to me,’ meaning the soul.—Then, as to the 
renewal of the soul. Here you must bear in mind that what 
is new is near to its beginning. The fount of the soul is the 
eternal outpouring from the Father’s heart, formally that is. 
But causally all, the whole (?) Trinity is the source; not 
the Persons in their separate reflections but in their perfect 
community of essence with, besides, the purpose of the will of 
God as dictated by the eternal law of wisdom, else were the 
soul eternal like her source, which the faith denies. God says 
he will draw the soul to him, and she shall be made new, for 
she has wandered far from him and in the death-throes of 
unlikeness caused by the assaults of lethal things has lost her 
pristine bloom, her first God-given freshness. Then the soul 
_ can say in spirit with the prophet, ‘ My youth is renewed like 
the eagle’s and my life is blossoming afresh in God.’ This is 
due to the precept and the practice of the seven virtues, the 
six works of mercy, the eight beatitudes and the twelve fruits 
of the Holy Ghost. Up then, glad soul, renew yourself! You 
can do it in this world. Not that God’s plan is this alone, for 
in this life there is not yet finished even the first festival of 
dedication ; its pattern of perfection is in the exalted Godhead. 
There God renews the (soul ?), restoring her to her original 

136 MEISTER ECKHART 

rank, and purifies her powers from all traces of mortality and 
sets her in the light of glory. Then God renews the soul so 
that the soul beholds herself in the pure being of God : herself, 
I say, as herself, blessed and really existent, natural and 
spiritual that is to say. Thereafter she beholds herself not as 
herself: she sees herself in God as eternal and divine.—Thirdly, 
God finally renews the soul when in ever new fresh-springing 
glory he presents himself to the intellect, and to desire as 
longing ‘fulfilled, so the soul is ever thirsting and at the same 
time drinking. Then the soul is dowsed in divine nature, then 
she receives divine light. 

XVIII ~ 

Si quis vult etc. God has poured all creatures out of himself 
in their natural state so that they might flow back in floods of 
glory. God has shown people his natural form (exemplary 
nature), his Son, so that in trying to copy him they may be 
led to the habit of the thought-free mind, to the first intel- 
ligible essence.! And since the very highest grade of perfection 
depends on the freeing of the mind from capricious variations 
and from creature consolations, therefore God says, ‘ Si quis 
vult etc. If any man will come after me let him deny himself 
and take up his cross and follow me.’ This saying is taken in 
four ways. According to Gregory and Origen, that man denies 
himself who from stubbornness changes to perfect humility ; 
from greed to despisery of earthly things, and who, foregoing 
his own will, endeavours to promote the will of God rather 
than his own felicity. In the book called The Well of Life it 
tells of a disciple questioning his master about escaping death 
if only he could find the well of life. The master said, ‘ Thou 
hast to escape from everything the outward senses perceive 
and in the summit of thy soul lift thyself up above thyself into 
the things which transcend all change, which no time can touch. 

1 The ‘ intelligible essences ’ or ‘ exemplary natures’ of the Schoolmen 
correspond to Plato’s Ideas. The ‘ first intelligible essence ’ is the divine 
essence, the ‘ beatific vision ’ of which is the goal of life: the return of man 
to his source. 

SERMONS 187 

Then thou shalt offer thyself up without reserve to the generous 
dispenser of all boons. When this work is finished in thee thou 
art in the well of life.’—John of the golden mouth, again, 
alluding to this saying lays it down that ‘ whosoever would 
be saved must first be purged by suffering ; then he must die 
to all things, and she (the soul) has to die to all things.” Gregory 
_ says, a man can have no notion of the things that are above 
him until he has some notion of what he is himself. And 
Augustine says what amounts to the same thing: ‘If a man 
wants his soul to be full of the light let him first heal the 
inner eye of the soul.’ After that he must cultivate this salutary 
‚state if the eye of the soul is to stand the light from above. 
Thirdly, the eye must never rove or rest elsewhere excepting 
in this light, nor in this light see anything except the light 
itself. But anyone who turns within before his sight is clear 
will be repelled, for this light blinds weak eyes. That is why 
King David wished for a pure heart and after that for the 
Holy Spirit which is given to none unless his inmost thoughts 
have been withdrawn and are far removed from all desire of 
mortal things. This condition is followed by the fourth, that 
now it is not just a stab but an abiding in the ineffable light 
where one really sees and knows it is only an illusion, all the 
sun is shining on. But I think we can find a deeper meaning 
in this saying, if you will follow me. God is pure being, and 
there is nothing in him other than pure being. If I say, then, 
God is God or good or kind or wise or anything else that I can 
predicate of God, it all amounts to nothing more than if I said, 
God is. For God’s being, which I mean when I say God is, 
is his divinity, his eternity, his wisdom, which I am speaking 
of when I affirm that God is good or wise. Therefore he told 
Moses, ‘Say, He who is hath sent me.’ Anything that falls 
short of God is by that fact something. I cannot now just 
say of it, it is. If I say it is an angel then by calling it an 
angel I deny it any other nature that other creatures have. 
And the more so since an angel has his own specific nature and 
is a natural descent from God. Yet by reason of nearness to 

138 MEISTER ECKHART 

God each angel possesses all that is possible to his natural 
grade of perfection, and hence it comes about that to every 
grade of angelic nature there belongs one angel. So many 
grades so many angels, neither more nor less. And here we 
note the marvellous adornment of the angels and the perfection 
of virtues which is theirs owing to their natural proximity to 
God. Yet as being inferior to God they are something, some- 
what that has essence (species). Human nature also is inferior 
to God, but a man is not a species because man is more inferior 
to God than angelic nature (is). No mere man is able to 
possess all that is possible to his grade of human nature. It 
follows that in any grade of human nature there are many 
men at once. So if I belong to it, and a certain person and 
another one, then the essence (species) will belong to each. 
But the essence common to all creatures is an outflowing of 
the pure fount of the divine essence and divine nature which 
is being itself. And since everything that flows out is trying 
to get back to its source therefore angel and man must deny 
their own being and cast themselves into the being which, 
against nature, is fighting its way back to God, whence it 
flowed out. Together man and angel come running back to 
God, bare of temporal accretions. Well then may it be said, 
* Whosoever will come to me, to my pure divine being, let him 
free himself from himself, what he is, and follow him from 
whom he is.” Anyone who puts this Saying into practice has 
gotten within reach of the divine treasure of spiritual perfection, 
and by pursuing it he will be led to the Sovran Good that has 
neither end nor beginning. Amen. 

XIX 1 

Mary Magdalene sat at the feet of our Lord and listened to 
his words. Martha said, ‘ Lord, dost thou not care that I am 
busy ? Bid her help me.’ Christ answered, ‘ But one thing 
is needful for eternal happiness, to behold, enjoy and suffer 
God.’ God would not be happy were he not speaking the 

1 This seems to be a collection of Meister Eckhart’s sayings. 

SERMONS 139 

Eternal Word. Christ would not be happy were he not hearing 
the Eternal Word. Creatures’ being is all a matter of doing. 
When they stop doing they stop being. God’s doing is his 
being. Were he not working he would not be existing. His 
working is his speaking. God can do nothing but speak the 
Eternal Word. If we are to be we must do and our doing is 
hearing the Eternal Word. The soul of Christ was happy only 
because of hearing the Eternal Word. Christ was united with 
the Godhead : God was man, man God, as we might say. But 
surely, had a single drop of Deity flowed into his body his body 
would be deathless : man could not have put God to death, 
The body of Christ was united, and this union could not give 
him that. Christ, without doing, would be as imperfect as I. 
It is my happiness to have God speaking in me. When God 
is speaking he is working and when he is working he is speaking. 
God is happy inasmuch as he is saying the Eternal Word. 
Seneca says, ‘ Believe me, no one is happy and rich.’ Rich of 
this world is not happy, man serves his possessions. If I serve 
riches I am in service. In service my freedom is lost and I 
am not happy. In the service of God a man enjoys all things 
in God. If I am not my own master I am not happy. That 
man is his own master on whom creatures have not enough 
hold to sway him for good or for ill. Unhappy is the man 
who has no power to draw upon his treasure in God. My 
happiness is nothing else than clearly seeing and hearing and 
experiencing God. Mary listened to his words. If I am to 
hear the Eternal Word things must all be silent in me. 

Perfection in each creature means the silence of the rest. 
For God to speak in me creatures must hold their peace. If 
thou hast anything speaking in thee God will say nothing : 
everything must have stopped speaking. Where God is 
present things have all said their say. Into him who is full of 
God there can get nothing at all. Paul says, ‘ Your life shall 
be like a lamp, wherein shines eternal life.’ I must keep this 
to myself: touching the naked Godhead Iam dumb, If Iam 
made in the image of God his image must be in me. The Word 

140 MEISTER ECKHART 

in which I am made like God 1 speak not: God says it in me. 
The prophet cries, in the Psalms, ‘ Open thy mouth and I will 
fill it.’ Nature lets nothing be empty. If nature acts thus 
with natural things then open the mouth of thy soul and it 
must perforce fill with God.—Thirdly, the Eternal Word must 
be silent in itself. The profoundest happiness lies in the mere 
beholding of the naked Godhead. If aught speaks in thee 
God says naught. By creature speaking in thee I mean thou 
holdest something dear enough for it to bias thee towards good 
or ill. All creatures are silent in the Godhead. The Trinity 
is silent in itself. In the Eternal Word the Father is not © 
‘speaking. In the Eternal Word nothing speaks but absolute 
essence. Were God’s Persons to end he would still subsist as 
pure being. My happiness lies in seeing God with God. God, 
speak, I shall hear. Do, I shall suffer. Show in the Eternal 
Word and I shall see. Let us be still and praise God, etc. 

XX 

He of Sterngassen said, a pure heart is better than knowledge 
or love, and how do I know this ? he asks. Let us see. 

Of loving people numbers have fallen, of clever people 
_ numbers have fallen: no pure heart ever fell. ‘ Using love,’ 
says one of the saints, ‘I find God unseizable, using intellect 
I find him unfathomable, but in the freedom of my pure abstract 
nature I find God mine in proportion to my detachment.’—We 
read of a wise man going to a God-lover and asking him how 
he fared. He answered, ‘I am in sore travail, always afraid, 
ever in dread of losing my love. And the more I love the less 
faith I have in my love.’ He went on and found a God-conner 
and asked him too how he fared. He said he was always 
working: ‘The more I know the more I find I do not know; 
the deeper I go the less I understand.” He went on again and 
found a man pure in heart. Him also he asked how he fared. 
Quoth he, ‘ The only thing I can say is that in God I have got 
all I want.” And by the same token the wise man exclaims, 
“I will be still and let God work in me; I will be silent and 

SERMONS 141 

hear what God says in me; I shall take refuge in detachment, 
so shall I find God more intimate with me.’ 

_ He said, I am asked what I mean by putting purity above 
understanding, above love and above grace. I will tell you. 
Whatever of good these three things have in them I find all 
at once in the pure heart and of better quality than as possessed 
by each one separately. How do Imakethis out? See. Love 
makes God dear to me ; purity makes me dear to God. Under- 
standing makes me see God ; purity makes me like God. Grace 
makes me God-worthy ; purity unites and puts me on a level 
with him. 

Also he defined what is meant by a pure heart : it will admit 
nothing at all, it abandons the world. So they praise as 
beautiful the woman in the story who, plucked out her own 
eyes and took them to her lover, saying, ‘Have for thine own 
that which in me delights thee and let me rest in my pure heart.’ 
That heart is pure wherein there is nothing to discard or to 
expel, wherein nothing appears to offend the clear eye of the 

mind. | ’ 

And he said what a godly man has to do. He has to close 
his heart and outward senses to external things and his inward 
senses to all mortal cares. He must turn his whole attention 
into himself. He must be still and listen to what God is saying | 
in him. He must hoist himself up above himself. He has got 
to be a mirror of divine ideas, to fill his soul with divine forms, 
_ He must see the light in the light, foster the light in the light, 

_ become the light in the light. He must have in the world no 

' more than his body. He must begin to explore eternity, must 

always be breaking fresh ground, always cultivating some new 
perception. i 8 

Further, he described a deiform man as one whose soul is 
filled with divine forms, all divine forms being formless, he 
said, and from formless forms flow infinite longings which can 
in no wise be expressed though three things are wrought by 
_ them in the soul : dispassionate love, direct knowledge, enlight- 
enment without end. 

142 MEISTER ECKHART 

He said, one who wants to have all things must set store by 
none. It is not what goes into the mouth that: defiles us but 
that which comes out, that defiles us. 

Pious folks must have threefold discrimination. First, the 
understanding has to be sufficiently acute to give a clear 
and detailed view of the part that nature plays in whatever 
may befall them, so that they may realize it fully and discount 
it. Next, whatever they may have to do they must always 
see whether it is right in principle.—Thirdly, their intelligence 
must be so subtile that they are able to discern, in the case 
of any inspiration or the very faintest light that is revealed 
to them, whether it comes from God or from some false 
spirit. 

XXI 

He of Sterngassen said too, as the prophet says in the psalter, 
all creatures ask me, Who is God ? Then I entered into myself 
and knew that creatures in themselves are nothing but a passing 
show. I saw that in the Godhead every creature is faultless 
bliss, and found that the light of God’s countenance was formed 
in me, and realized that endless joy was locked within my 
breast and that there was in me a diapsalm and unbroken still- 
ness of all interior things and heartfelt sighs to heaven; and 
my mind was free from images and my spirit free from means 
and the object of my thought was bared and the person of my 
mind was changed. There entered into me a me forgot in 
thee, and my reason was inspired into thee, and by thy Holy 
Spirit I was led into the ground, wherein the Son is imaged, 
and I saw there God in God and the Father’s nature in the Son 
and the Son’s Person in the Father and the Holy Ghost’s Person 
in the Father and in the Son. Then I had a higher sight, a 
higher longing (will), a higher understanding. I found in me 
all things forgotten, my own self forgotten and awareness of 
thee God alone. There came in me a vision of thy eternity and 
a sense of thy felicity, and I found myself simply gazing upon 
thee. I found myself in nature just-like thee, I found myself 

SERMONS 143 

in nature one with thee, I found myself let into thy eternity 
and found that all thy bliss thou hadst poured into me. I found 
myself with thee being thy being and speaking the Word and 
breathing the Spirit ; and in my soul the Father was almighty, 
the Son all-knowing, and the Holy Ghost all-loving. O Eternal 
Light of Divine Glory, since thou art in my innermost depths, 
since thou transcendest all things, be to me that thou art, a 
turning away from all things into the ineffable Good that thur. 
art in thy naked self. 

XXI 

Unchangeableness and complete detachment from creatures, 
that sets me nearest to God and to the summit of perfection. 
Purity makes me all unmindful of things, and by purity God 
is always kept in me. Purity makes me uniform with God, 
purity forces me into the inner being of creatures, purity 
means farewell to all creatures. None but the pure in heart 
can see God. In purity only is God to be found. Intellect 
teaches me about things, purity makes me see God. By 
purity God is made captive in me, purity makes me God- 
conscious and conscious of naught beside God, purity begets 
detachment. The pure soul has a light-birth as it were, purity 
is satisfied with God alone. 

XXIII 

He of Sterngassen said, I am often asked what our Lady was 
doing when the angel came to her. Twenty-one things are 
predicated of her. She was sitting in time untimely. She 
was sitting, a creature uncreaturely. She was sitting in the 
body maidenly. Her soul was deiform. Her spirit was con- 
templating God. Her mind was heavenly calm. Her outward 
life was altogether lovely. Her soul was generous. To her 
nothing at all under God was of any account. Her heart was 
aflame with the truth. Clear consciousness was her school. 
Heaven was her cell. Divinity was her reading. Eternal 
truth was her mentor. She had no inclination towards 

144 MEISTER ECKHART 

creatures but was simply bent upon God. ‚She was liberated 
from creatures and set upon God alone. Her spirit was inspired 
with the Spirit of God. In body on the earth her spirit was . 
in heaven. She was in the land of freedom. Where lies the 
land of freedom? In complete detachment from creatures, 
and in the land of freedom lies the state of purity. She sat in 
the cell of recollection and dispassion. She sat in the house 
of godly certitude. She sat in the school of the Trinity and 
heard what the schoolmaster of truth was saying. In the 
darkness she saw a light. In the silence she heard a word. 
In passivity she was aware of activity. Her soul was all the 
time resting in eternity and abiding in the Godhead and she 
was satisfied with the divine perfection. 

XXIV 

It is a saint who says, ‘ This is sanctity, to know what we 
were before time and what we are in time and what we shall 
be after time.’ He of Sterngassen has spoken very finely on 
these three. He said on one occasion, ‘ He has formed us in 
him and with him.’ He has formed us in him. Mark how 
he has formed us. We are a light in his brightness and a word 
in his understanding and a life in his interior nature. Thus 
he formed us in him before time. Next, what we are now in 
time. In us is a light into which without ceasing the light 
of the Godhead is shining. In us is a mind into which without 
ceasing the Word of the Trinity is speaking. And in us is a 
spiritual nature wherein without ceasing the life of eternity 
is working. Thirdly, what we shall be after time. We shall 
be absorbed into God essentially, confusedly, completely. How 
shall we be absorbed into God essentially ? In seeing, not in 
being. His being will not be our being, it will be our life, 
as Christ says, ‘To know thee Father and thy Son Jesus 
Christ, this is eternal life.’ He did not say it was eternal 
being. 

Peradventure you will say, But, Sir, if God has implanted 

SERMONS 145 

in our souls such nobility, such riches, if the light of his deity 
is shining in the purity of my soul and the Word of the Trinity 
is speaking in me and the life of eternity is energizing in my 
interior nature, then why am I so unaware of it? Thou dost 
ask me this? But I have already told thee it comes of being 
so scattered, of behaving just as though ye were unconnected, 
self-estranged, and reckless of invading creature images. Why 
are there so many who cannot understand me? ‘I will tell you. 
Simply because they have not given up their lives to him. 
When I speak of unity how should he understand me whose 
heart is all to pieces in multiplicity ? And similarly with 
eternity, I say, how should he understand me who finds his 
satisfaction in time and temporal things ? When I talk of a 
pure heart how can a person understand me who harbours 
all and sundry in his inmost soul? For it is not enough to be 
rid of creatures, having them, we must oust as well their 
capricious images which so heavily handicap the soul. Alas, 
alas, if only ye could realize how ye are hindering yourselves 
and how much of eternal truth ye might understand by dint 
of application and guarding well the inner man! Ye are afraid 
of your own selves. Believe me ye might know.as much and 
more than I do. Why is it then that I know more than you 
“of God? Not because of more book-knowledge, book-learning 
will hardly help at all. The reason is that ye have not as 
carefully as I have detached and freed yourselves entirely from 
things. Were ye as dead to things and held them as imper- 
manent as I do then ye would know as much as I know, very 
likely more. Besides, I left myself not only in pursuit of grace, 
but also I was minded to arrive at wisdom. I follow my own 
lights because you see I find I am cognisant of things that are 
hidden from those who have not made the effort to be free 
and escaped from the net of the temporal illusion of striving 
creatures. As well might God consort with devil as join him- 
self to any soul while yet she is not wholly free from fugitive 
emotions and from creature images. Whenever the soul shall 
loose her hold and cease to concern herself with time and 

146 MEISTER ECKHART 

temporalities she will be able for great things. You must know 
that in her works she follows hard upon the heels of God. 
Why is it such a number of even learned men can hardly 

bear that we should set the soul so near to God and credit her _ 

with being so like God? It simply means they do not know 
the exalted lineage of the soul. If they fully realized her high 
descent they would cavil at the various points of difference they 
must find between the soul and God. 

Will you ask God to help me and protect me in one matter 
I am greatly exercised about ? You must know that I have 
spent much thought upon it and am still concerned about it 
more than I care to say or can well tell you, albeit love con- 
strains me, remembering my joy in you.—I wonder and have 
wondered a long time why the soul cannot speak as powerful 
a word as the heavenly Father can. According to some doctors 
it is because in God that is essential which is merely imaged 
in the soul. She cannot do as the Father can, because she has 
not gotten it in reality. This explanation does not satisfy me. 
Other doctors tell us what God is. His being and existence 
he gets from himself, but the soul is from God, that she is, and 
therefore in her works she will not compare with God. This 
is quite unconvincing, for the Son too has gotten from the 
Father all he is and he acts the same as the Father. One 
short statement, however, yields a clue. It is that the Son 
is born out of the Person of the Father while remaining in 
him in essence. As immanent in him he is able therefore to 
do all the Father does, both as Person and essence. But the 
soul proceeded from the Person and also from the essence, 
and since she does not remain in the essence therefore she does 
not act like the Father. With this explanation I am satisfied 
to some extent, not altogether. 

XXV 

“In all things I have sought rest and in nothing have I 
found it.’ She says, I have found rest in nothing but naught. 
The naught the soul finds rest in is the naked Godhead. Sick 

SERMONS 147 

people have fancies for all sorts of things, especially women 
big with child. And the soul which has conceived and is 
pregnant with God’s Word is full of longings. Sometimes she 
is longing to be rid of creatures, sometimes to ‘stamp out her 
own nature, and sometimes she is craving for excursions in the 
Trinity, but rest she cannot find except in naught. As our 
Lord says, ‘Let thy dwelling be in Jacob.’ Jacob signifies a 
wrestler. He means then that to rest in God man must be 
always struggling with and overcoming things. If I have 
overcome myself I have overcome all things. That man has 
overcome all things whom naught can overcome to make him 
stoop to imperfection. 

As a thing is in its essence so does it act. My soul is.deiform 
in her essence, therefore she is omnipotent and her work is 
eternal. All God can do she can bear. He can do no more 
nor less. Our doctors can none of them tell which is the’ 
greater, God’s power or the soul’s capacity. Since, then, I 
have omnipotence I must never stop till I have gotten all 
things, less than all being less than I. To the suffering soul 
beholding God all things are too narrow, too confined, which 
makes me wonder how a soul who finds the whole of things too 
small is able to rest in any single creature. Because I am 
important to myself therefore trivialities are important to me. 
Were I nothing to myself things would be nothing to me. 
Seneca the philosopher says, Who fears God everything fears. 
Who fears not God fears everything. He who fears God is 
friends with him. To be friends with God is to be on his side. 
On God’s side one can do all things. 

By nature things rest when they find their own place. Beasts 
rest when their wants are satisfied. Each creature rests in its 
own place. Pick up a stone and throw it in the air, it never 
rests till it falls back to earth. Why is that ? Because earth 
is its fatherland, air its land of exile. Everything comes to 
rest in the place where it was born. The place I was born in is 
the Godhead. The Godhead is my fatherland. Have I got 
a father in {he Godhead? Not only have I got a Father there 

148 MEISTER ECKHART 

but I have got myself. Ere I was in myself I was born in the 
Godhead. If once God’s Word were spoken in me nothing in 
the world would stop me climbing right up into the Godhead. 
When anything stays long away from its own place it spoils. 
Throw a bird into the water and it drowns. Throw a fish into 
the air, it perishes. The fish is born in water, water is its 
nature. Being born in God wilt thou live out of God? Surely 
then thou diest. 

If thy wont is temporal I shall never believe that thou art 
spiritual. If thy life is spiritual thy wont will be godly con- 
templation.—Eternal freedom simply means being freed from 
self.—The soul has a deiform nature, for she is made in the 
image of God. She has an omnipotent nature, for what God 
has in eternity in uncreatedness she has in time and creaturely. 
—Nothing can satisfy me but something that will fill me. 
The man who is hungry for God has no stomach for aught 
beside God. If I desire things they are desirable. They are 
not desirable in themselves. Were I full of God I should care 
nothing whatever for the world. To respect the world shows 
want of self-respect. Self-respect betokens despisery of things. 
Rest is complete loss of motion. Were any creature perfectly 
immoveable it would be God. God is God in that he is im- 
moveable. Creatures are all of them moveable and restless, 
therefore I cannot rest except in God. Intelligent creatures 
rest only in their own (intellectual) activity. What is the end 
of my activity? What in God is a doing shall in me be a 
suffering. What in God is a speaking shall in me be a hearing. 
What in God is a form shall be a vision in me. 

XXVI 

In this sermon Meister Eckhart sets out the great benefit of the eoming 
of our Lord: before, all people had to go to hell, but now God is not hard 
to please, a pure heart is enough. 

Ecce dies veniunt, dicit dominus, et suscitabo David germen 
vustum (Jer. 995). The words of J eremiah, ‘Behold the days 
come, saith the Lord, that I will quicken the true root of 

SERMONS 149 

David [and the fruit thereof shall be wise and shall judge 
justly and bring righteousness on earth].’ Solomon says, 
* Like water to a thirsty soul are good tidings from a foreign 
land.’ In the matter of his sins man is far from God, so heaven 
is (to him) a far and foreign land and Jeremiah was a messenger 
from heaven. St Augustine tells about himself that before he 
was converted he found himself far away from God in a strange 
and alien country. It is a lamentable thing for anyone to be 
away from what he cannot be happy without. If the fairest 
of the creatures God has made were fetched out of the light in 
which they stand—and so far as things are standing in the 
light of God so far they are happy and at ease—suppose, I say, 
it were God’s will, he did so ordain, that they were taken out of 
the light divine and assigned to any [the least] soul, she would 
find no pleasure in them, she would be dismayed. Mere 
lamentable is the case of one who is away from him apart 
from whom he cannot be at all. And worst of all to be away 
from him who is his eternal bliss [and grown infirm so that not 
by any power of his own can he return to God nor does he 
know even where he may find him. David bewails this, 
saying, ‘I am far from God, for I am born in sin and am become 
so frail that by no virtue of my own can I get back to God, 
withal my sight is gone so that I cannot tell if I encounter 
him again.’ A good man said in John’s book, ‘ One person at 
variance with another will probably find someone to put 
matters straight; but being at variance with God who is so 
high, so unapproachable, none can be found to heal the breach, 
no man, the noblest of them; more masses are his only hope]. 
So it was good news the prophet gave, ‘ Behold the days come, 
etc.’ When the patriarchs realized their parlous state they 
cried aloud to heaven, and casting them in spirit into God let 
God in godly wisdom be born [of human nature, (God) who 
shall save us from our miserable plight]. This message was 
as good as a drink of cold water to a thirsty soul. Moreover, 
it is true that God will give his kingdom for a draught of cold - 
water, 2nd this, with a kind heart, is enough, and whoso offers 

150 MEISTER ECKHART 

thanks in eternal love (wherein God was made man), he shall 
be saved. Man durst not fear the devil nor the world nor his 
own flesh and our Lord God as well [he need not fear. For] as 
St Paul explains, ‘ the Son is given us for advocate, who is the 
wisdom of his Father, and he will make excuse for all our 
follies and misdeeds.’ St Paul says also, ‘ He is given to be 
our champion,-to fight our battles for us at all times of need. 
Though the Father should want to fight against us he would 
not be able to, for the very power and wisdom which the Father 
has the Son has just the saine, who is specially charged with 
our defence and has won us much too dearly to want to let us 
go, withal the Father cannot say him nay since he is his wisdom 
nor can he fight against ‘him since he is his power. So man 
need have no fear, he can always boldly go to God. 

When man was driven out of paradise God put three keepers 
[at the gate]. One was angelic nature, another was a flaming 
sword, the third its being double-edged. Angelic nature stands 
for purity. [Which man has not got.] When God’s Son came 
on earth, who was his (clear) and flawless mirror, [the face and 
reflection of his heavenly Father wherein God’s will is plainly 
seen], he broke through the outer guard and brought innocence 
and purity in[to] human nature upon earth. 

The flaming sword stands for divine fiery love without which 
none can get to heaven. This Christ brought with him and 
forced the second guard, for with that very love he had loved 
mankind ere he created him. Jeremiah says, ‘ God has loved 
thee with eternal love.’ 

The third guard, the sharp sword, was human misery. Of 
this our Lord has shouldered the whole load, as Isaiah says, 
vere languores etc. ‘Surely he shall bear our griefs (and carry 
our sorrows).’ He came to earth on purpose to take men’s sins 
upon himself and wipe them out and save mankind. Now 
heaven is thrown wide. So man can boldly approach God. 

One thing more we note in his pronouncement, ‘I will 
quicken the seed of David’ (or, the fruit). You remember 
how at certain times the angel moved the water and it gained 

SERMONS | 151 

the power to heal the sick. A much more momentous thing 
was the moving of man’s nature in our Lady’s womb by the 
Son of God. By that all human nature has been healed. And 
it was a greater blessing still that God with (his) own nature 
did move the waters in (the) Jordan when he was baptized. 
Then he gave power to all water so that when a person is 
baptized he is cleansed from all his sins and becomes a child 
of God. Greatest of all blessings is God’s birth and revelation 
in the soul in spiritual union. Then a man is made more 
blessed than the body of our Lord Jesus Christ without his 
soul and without his godhood, any sainted soul being nobler 
than the mortal body of our Lord Jesus Christ [was]. 

The interior birth of God in the soul is the final consummation 
of her happiness, and the bliss of it is more to her than that of 
our Lord’s becoming man in our Lady’s womb or than his 
moving of the waters. Nothing God has ever wrought or done 
for man is any use at all except as uniting him with God in 
spiritual union wherein God is born within his soul and the soul 
is born in God, and to this end has God wrought all his works. 
Please God this is in store for us. Amen. 

XXVII? 

In this sermon Meister Eckhart shows by argument and by examples 
how God is born in the soul and the soul is born into God. 

In illo tempore missus est angelus Gabriel a deo. * Ave gratia 
plena, dominus tecum’ (Luc. 126). St Luke says, ‘ Then the 
angel was sent from God.’ When? * In the sixth month’ 
of John the Baptist’s being in his mother’s womb. 

If anyone asks me, Why do we pray, why do we fast, why 
do we work, why are we baptized, why—most important of all 
—did God become man? I answer, that God may be born in 
the soul and the soul be born into God. All the scriptures have 
been written, God has made the world and all angelic nature 
in order that God might be born in the soul and the soul be 

1 See also Jostes, Appendix 1, No. 4, and Pfeiffer, I, xxix and xxviii. 
Büttner’s modern German version, vol. ii, No. 1, is very free. 

152 MEISTER ECKHART 

born into God. All cereal nature means wheat, all metal
