nature means gold, all generation means ınan. As one philo- 
sopher observes, ‘ We can find no animal without some likeness 
to man.’ At the moment when this word is first conceived 
within my mind it is an abstract and very subtle thing, it is 
true word before it is imaged in my thought. Thirdly, it is 
spoken by the mouth, and then is nothing more than an exterior 
symbol for the interior word. Even so the eternal Word is 
spoken within in the heart of the soul, in her innermost, in her 
purest part, in the head of the soul, her intellect namely; 
therein happens this birth. Anyone who has merely the hope, 
the lively conviction of this, will be glad to know how this birth 
comes about and what conduces to it. 

“In the fullness of time God sent his Son,’ says St Paul. 
St Augustine explains what is meant by this fullness of time. 
‘Where time is no more there is fullness of time. When the 
day is completed the day is done. And it is of course true that 
there is no time where this birth befalls, for nothing hinders 
this birth so much as time and creature. Assuredly time 
cannot touch either God or the soul by nature. If the soul 
could be touched by time she would not be soul and if God 
could be touched by time he would not be God. Further, if 
time could touch the soul God could never be born in her (and 
she could never be born in God). For God to be born in the 
soul time must fall away altogether or she must escape from 
time by willing or longing. 

Another meaning of fullness of time. Suppose someone had 
the knowledge and the power to sum up in the present now, 
all the time and the happenings in that time of six thousand 
years, including everything to come until the end, that would 
be fullness of time. That is the now of eternity, where the soul 
in God knows all things new and fresh and pleasing like the 
ones that are present to me here. I was reading in a little 
book that, if we did but know it, God still goes on making the 
world just as he did the first day. Herein is God rich and this 
is God’s kingdom. The soul that God is born in will be lost to 

SERMONS 153 

time, she shall escape out of time, and rising up stand staring 
into the kingdom of God. There wide has no width and broad 
has no breadth. _ There the soul knows all things and knows 
them in perfection. Philosophers speaking of the size of 
heaven (say it is incredible). Yet the smallest of the powers 
in my soul is more than heaven wide. To say nothing of the 
intellect : it is capacious beyond measure. In the head of 
the soul, in the intellect, therein I am as near a place more 
than two thousand leagues beyond the seas as (I am) to the 
spot I stand on now. In this open, this kingdom of God, the 
‚soul knows. Nothing escapes her, and there she has nothing 
to wait for. 

‘The angel was sent.’ According to the masters the angel 
hosts are countless. They are too numerous for number to 
contain; their number cannot even be conceived. To one 
who knows distinction apart from time and number an hundred 
would be the same as one. Were there an hundred Persons in 
the Godhead the man who sees distinction apart from time and 
number would apprehend no more than one. Unbelievers are 
surprised at this and some unlettered Christians too, nay there 
are some priests even who know no more about it than a stone. 
They take (the) three (Persons) like three cows or three stones, 
whereas anyone who can see in God distinction apart from time 
and number knows the three Persons are one God. 

An angel is a most exalted being. According to the best 
authorities each of the angels has a whole nature to itself. 
Imagine a man gifted with everything that men have ever had, 
have now and ever shall have of power, wisdom and the rest, 
that man would be a marvel, but he would still be reckoned 
as no more than one man and far from an angel at thet. Simi- 
larly every single angel has one entire nature, and one differs 
from another as one beast does from another of another nature. 
In this multitude of angels God is rich, and to know it is to 
realize something of God’s kingdom. It proclaims God rich 
just as a lord is argued rich from the number of his knights. 
Therefore he is called by us the Lord of Hosts. These hosts 

154 MEISTER ECKHART 

of angels, however exalted they may be, all their part 
when God is born inthe soul; that is to say, they enjoy, rejoice 
and delight in the birth. They do none of the work. There 
is no creaturely activity. God brings about this birth by him- | 
self, the angels’ work being one of service. Whatever ministers 
thereto is work of service. 

The angel was called Gabriel. He did what he was called. 
His name was no more Gabriel than Conrad. No one can 
know an angel’s name. Into where the angels bear name no 
master or mind ever entered. Peradventure he is nameless, 
Nor has the soul any name. We can no more find the real 
name of the soul than we can find the proper name for God, 
in spite of weighty books that are written on it. But where 
she peers out in her works she is called after them. The 
carpenter, that is not his name, it is the name he gets from the 
work of which he is master. And Gabriel is the name he takes 
from his work of messenger, Gabriel meaning power, energy. 
In this birth God works powerfully, he energizes. What is all 
the power of nature for? To bring forth itself. Why is every 
nature trying to bring forth? In order to re-produce itself. 
The nature of my father is trying to produce a father in his 
nature. Since this cannot be it tries to make one exactly like 
him. Failing this it makes the nearest thing it can, which is 
a son. And where the power is still weaker or some accident 
befalls, it makes a yet more different person. Now since there 
is perfect power in God therefore he makes his match in this 
birth. What God is in power, in wisdom, in truth, all this he 
gets into the soul. 

St Augustine says, ‘What the soul loves she grows like.’ 
By loving things earthly she will grow earthly, and if she loves 
God does she then, we might ask, become God? Were I to 
Say so it would sound incredible to people of intelligence too 
poor to understand. St Augustine says, ‘It is not I who say 
so, I refer you to the scriptures, where it says: “I have said, 
ye are gods.”’ Anyone possessing something of those riches I 
was talking of just now—a glimpse, a hope perhaps, or a firm 

_ SERMONS 155 

conviction, will understand quite well. Never was there born 
anything so near akin, so like, so much the same as God as 
the soul becomes in this birth. If it is hampered in some way 
so that she cannot grow exactly like, that is not God’s fault. 
_So far as her shortcomings are repaired he will make her like 
himself. No carpenter can build a solid house out of rotten 
wood. That is no fault of his, the fault is in the wood. And 
so with God’s work in the soul. Were the lowest of the angels 
to take shape or be born in the soul this whole world would 
count for naught—seeing that in one lone angelic spark there 
grows and thrives and shines everything in the world—but this 
birth is the work of God himself. No angel can do anything 
to help, nothing but work of service. 

‘Ave.’ That means, free from woe. Freedom from creature 
means freedom from sorrow, freedom from hell,.and those who, 
possess and like creatures least have the least sorrow, It is 
a favourite saying with me, He who loves the world least 
possesses it most. None owns the world like the man who has 
left it. What, think ye, makes God God? His freedom from 
creatures. He is unmindful of them in time. In time is 
creature (and sin) and death. These are in a sense akin, so 
whenever the soul escapes out of time she escapes from sorrow 
and pain, aye, hardship is then her joy. No imaginable 
happiness, joy, loveliness, or bliss is any happiness at all com- 
pared with the bliss of this birth. 

‘Full of grace.’ The smallest work of grace is of more 
exalted nature than any of the angels. St Augustine says, 
any act of grace that is wrought by God, the conversion of a 
sinner for example, and making him into a good man, is a 
thing of greater moment than God’s creating a new world. 
It is just as easy for God to make the heavens and the earth 
turn round as for me to turn an apple in my hand. Where 
grace is present in the soul she is as pure, as like, as much 
akin to God—grace being free from activity—as in this birth, 
wherein, as I was saying, there is no activity. Grace does no 
work. St John gave never a sign. The work of an angel in 

156 | MEISTER ECKHART 

God is so exalted, no master or mind can conceive it. But 
from that work there falls a chip, as a chip might fall in build- 
ing a house : a flash where, as his lightest task, the angel sets , 
_ the heayens rolling. Everything in the world lives, grows and 

> thrives because of this. I sometimes speak of two founts— 

perhaps that sounds strange, still we must use such words as 
fit our thoughts—one fount, whence grace springs, where the 
Father brings forth his only Son: at this point starts grace 
which issues in this same stream. The other fount is the one 
in which creatures flow out of God; it is as far from the fount 
where grace springs as heaven is from the earth. Grace does 
“no work. Ordinary fire as existing in its nature does not 
burn and is quite harmless; the heat of the fire is what burns, 
but this heat, even, in the fire-nature, will not burn and does 
no damage. Truly the heat which is present in the fire and the 
actual fire-nature are heaven and earth apart. Grace does no 
work. It is too fine for that. Work is as alien to grace as 
heaven to earth. A being in, being established in, being one 
with God, this is grace—and God too, for that follows. 

‘God with thee.’ This is the birth. Let no one think it is 
beyond him. What matters the hardship to me if he does the 
work ? All his behests I can easily obey. He may tell me to 
do whatever he likes, it will trouble me not in the least ; it 
is all quite simple and easy so long as he provides me with 
sufficient grace. Some people say they have not got it, and 
I answer: I am sorry, but art thou longing for it ?—‘ No.’ 
Then I am sorrier still. Though we may not have it we can 
at least desire it. David cries, ‘I have: longed to desire thy 
righteousness.’ Let us long for God-until he is pleased to be 
born in us. So help us, ete. 

XXVIII . 

Here Meister Eckhart teaches that when any soul is given the name 
Jesus this is always done on the eighth day. 

Post dies octo vocatum est nomen eius Jesus (Luc. 25). ‘On 
the eighth day his name was (called) Jesus.’ The name Jesus 

a SERMONS 157 
is not given except by inspiration of the Holy Breath. A 
venerable doctor lays it down that ‘ in whatever soul the name 
of Jesus shall be spoken this will be done on the eighth day.’ 
. On the first day he yields his own will to God’s will and lives 
. for that only. The next day there are glimmerings of the 
celestial fire. The third day corresponds with the tortured 
soul’s unceasing quest for God. The fourth day all the powers 
of the man are fixed on God. According to one authority, 
* When the soul is touched by eternal things she is moved, and 
by this motion she is warmed and by the warmth she is expanded 
and made very receptive of God.’ On the sixth day the soul is 
united to God. The seventh day God receives the soul. The 
eighth day is the enjoyment of God, and = the name Jesus 
is given to the child. 

XXIX . 

‘In this dleabutes Meister Eckhart shows that when room is made for 
_God then, where there is peace, God comes to the soul. 

Angelus domini apparuit etc. (Matt. 1,)). ‘The angel 
appeared to Joseph while he slept, and said to him, Take the 
child, etc.’ A wise man says the scriptures are in meaning 
much like running water which overflows at times and is used 
to fill the dykes and for baptizing yet flows on all the while. 
St Augustine says, ‘It is well that the scriptures should be 
obscure in meaning, so that the highest truth is not discovered 
at first glance.’ We find many happy and profitable sayings 
which are consistent with the highest truth, Moses’ statement, 
for example, that there is water over us as well as underneath. 
Who can tell? Pious men enquire why God made man last, 
after he had made all creatures. Possibly the fundamental 
reason and true explanation is that he made in man the epitome 
of creaturely perfections. Thus the Holy Trinity took counsel 
when about to make mankind, and said, ‘Let us make man in 
our image.’ From which it would appear that this image of 
the Holy Trinity is innate in the soul. Then, again, angelic 
nature, which she has in common with the angels, as well as all 
creaturely likeness and perfection, is natural to man, so that 

158 MEISTER ECKHART 

God beholds, sees mirrored, in mankind, his own perfection 
added to that of all creation, proof positive that man ranks 
highest among creatures. Moses made four books, very useful 
ones; afterwards he made a fifth, the smallest and the best 
which he called the truth of all the scriptures. This God and 
Moses had put in the,ark. St Augustine, too, made many 
books. Finally he also made a little book in which were 
- written all the things that in the others people could not 
understand. This, his favourite one, he always kept at hand. 
And so it is with man: God made him for a handbook which 
he dips into and toys with and takes pleasure in. That man 
commits a heinous sin who hampers this saving practice. On 
the day of judgment all creatures will clamour loudly against 
anyone who does so. 

This goes to show that after Herod’s death Joseph did right 
to go back to the land where those who hindered God had 
made room for him. Even so must sins make room for God 
if the soul is to be in the right state for God to dwell in. Accord- 
ing to St John, ‘ The true light came into the world and the 
world received it not.’ Meaning to say, it found no place to 
stay in and was not received on that account. It has been 
said, ‘If with pure heart thou wouldst receive and know thy 
God, then kill cut pleasure, hope and fear.’—This is the first 
step towards making room for God. The second one is peace, 
which was reigning in the land where God was born. This is 
shown by the fact that everyone belonged and was subject to 
one rule. Hic nota historiam. I prove it too by those three 
kings who came from distant lands. Perfect peace has to 
reign in the soul. There is real peace where the lowest is sub- 
ject to the highest. We pray, etc. 

XXX 

Here Meister Eckhart sets forth what Christ teaches us in the four 
knowledges. 

Sedebat Jesus doceus in templo (Luc. 19,,). The gospel says, 
Christ sat in the temple and taught. His sitting suggests calm, 

SERMONS 159 
and a person who is sitting down is more apt to consider serious 
things than one who is walking or standing about. Sitting 
symbolizes rest, standing activity, and going restlessness. So 
it behovesthe soul to sit, most patient and humble of creatures, 
and she shall enter into rest and peace. The peace she gets 
in a light. The light is given her in a stillness wherein she sits, 
abides. Albertus (says), ‘ Teachers sit down while they are 
teaching, because reclining lets the heavy humours, impure blood 
that is to say, mount into the brain and dull the understand- 
_ ing. But while a man is sitting his bad blood will flow down, 
and lighter humours rising to his head, memoria is illumined.’ 
This explains why Christ sat in the temple, 7.e. in the soul. 

The next thing is the ‘ teaching.’ What does he teach? He 
teaches understanding, the proper uses of it ; for whatever be 
the teacher, it teaches after its own nature. -Christ is under- 
standing, so he teaches us understanding. 

Christ had four. kinds of knowledge and wisdom. The 
first was divine. Therewith he knew the things in the eternal 
providence, not only what is and what shall be but also all. 
God could do if he would. With this light he could see into 
people’s hearts, and such acts as are proper to God he did in 
virtue of this knowledge. These things were possible to Christ 
in the wisdom which is God.—The second knowledge of Christ 
is creature, that (beatific) knowledge namely which was poured 
into his soul when she was created, and it is supernatural. 
In it she enjoyed God and saw God in his essence. In this 
knowledge he neither waxed nor waned. This knowledge gave 
him power to know all that God had ever done or was going to 
do, but nothing that concerns his (own) infernal nature: that 
she knows nothing about. This light is creature albeit super- 
natural to the soul.—The third (infused or angelic) knowledge 
he has in common with the angels who have in them universal 
ideas of things. Dionysius says, ‘ When God created the angels 
he gave them universal species, and these they have innately.’ 
So has Christ’s soul innately the form or exemplar of all things 
which he has given her; not that he is himself that form any 

160 MEISTER ECKHART 

more than the seal which gives its form to the wax is identical 
with it. In this knowledge he neither lost nor gained. By 
this means she was able to know all present happenings but 
not future ones, just as an angel has no knowledge of things to 
come unless it be revealed to him; it is not his by nature. — 
The fourth knowledge he had was of sensible things. What 
the senses receive from without is subtly conveyed to the image- 
maker (imagination), where mental insight falls upon it. In 
this case he progresses like ourselves. ‘ There is growth in the 
powers of the mind,’ as Doctor, or Brother, Thomas says. 

Let us see now what he teaches us in these knowledges. The 
first. knowledge, which is God, whence proceeded all things, in 
this he tells us how we shall-and must return and restore all 
things to their original source. This happens in the man in 
whom multiplicity is all gathered up and the whole raised into 
God, into the first cause, God namely. When a man has 
reached the point-of finding he is one with God, then, not till 
_ then will he restore all things to their original source. St 
Bernard cries, ‘Lord, what is man that thou hast loved him 
so?” ‘Blessed is he in whom all manifold things are reduced 
. to unity.’—This is what he taught us in the knowledge which is 
God. What does he teach us in the knowledge which is super- 
natural? He teaches us to transcend what is natural. We 
must first overcome our own senses, thoughts and opinions _ 
based upon them. Up, noble soul, put on thy jumping shoes, 
understanding and love, and leap the workings of thy powers : 
leap thine own understanding, the three hierarchies and the three 
heavens as well as the light which is energizing thee, and spring 
into the heart of God, into his hiddenness, wherein thou art 
hidden from all creatures. This is what he teaches us in super- 
natural knowledge. As St Paul says, ‘Ye are dead, and your 
life is hid with Christ in God.’—Then what does he teach us in 
the natural knowledge which he has in common with the angels 
who have general ideas in them? The soul too has the power 
of grasping generalities. And she ought to dwell in herself, 
seeing truth comes from within and not from without. ‘O 

SERMONS 161 

Lord,’ St Augustine exclaims, ‘ what numbers have gone out 
of themselves to look for the truth and have never come back 
into themselves again!’ They never found the truth simply 
because God is the core and centre of the soul.—This is what 
he teaches us in this supernatural knowledge. What does he 
teach us in the progressive knowledge? He teaches us the 
conduct of the outward man. This conduct is arrived at by 
self-examination, for to know oneself is better than knowledge 
of all created things. 

Christ taught them. Who are the ones he teaches? The 
simple ones. Who are really simple? Those who are 
harmless and never deceive but are at the same time never 
deceived: these are really simple. In this true simplicity 
help us, etc. 

XXXI 

In this sermon Meister Eckhart explains how God calls the soul to him 
by the creation of all creatures, and about the reward. 

Voca operarios et redde illis mercedem suam (Matth. 20,). 
In St Matthew it says, ‘ Call the labourers and pay them their 
hire.’ By this Lord who calls the labourers into his vineyard 
is meant our Lord who calls all people to him in two ways. 
First by the creation of all creatures, which he has made so 
noble and so fair; and secondly by death, death which falls 
so heavy on the sinner’s heart. About the first a master 
comments thus, * God made the world for man, and all things 
in the world, and mankind for himself, and the body he has 
(given) to the soul God has overcome and so made all creatures 
to show forth his glory to the soul while he is so intent on 
knowing how to attract the soul and lure her to his love that, 
everything in heaven and earth forgot, he only seeks to find 
out in what way each soul may besc be brought to him.’ (He) 
has made all kinds of creatures so that his glory may be shown 
in many different ways, albeit no creature did he make without 
something painful, some semblance of hell, for creatures are all 
messengers, beckoning to God, since they all are proclaiming the 
glory of God. 

162 MEISTER ECKHART 

Two things God tries on creatures. The first one, comfort, 
pleasure, he uses as a bait for man, while giving him to under- 
stand that it is at its height in God; and pain is the other 
thing he lays upon a creature, for instance, on a man who for 
the sake of rank or pleasure is careless about God. Marvellous 
as the minds of men are the wonderful ways of God. Where 
one is lured by pleasure another must be driven by sickness 
and misfortune, witness the astonishing conversion of St Paul. 
On his way to persecute the Christians God struck him down 
and encompassed him with his light; and St Augustine, when 
he was converted, the very day of it, could not get over his 
delight in the wonderful lure God had laid for his soul, to 
convert -her. God, who is one, has parcelled himself out 
among all creatures, so that the soul can in no way turn from 
God to creature without finding his likeness therein. No 
-sinner would ever revel in sin were not God’s likeness somehow 
present there, as honour, comfort, pleasure. And hence it is 
that many a man is willing to give up both goods and friends 
while honour he refuses to forgo, his honour which lies nearest 
to his heart, and in this he follows God most closely, for God 
says, * Mine honour will I give to none.’ As the commentary 
on the Psalter says, ‘ No one is willing to sacrifice his honour 
to save the honour of his friend.’ 

There are three reasons why the soul can get no satisfaction 
out of creatures. In the first place they are partial: drink 
enough is not food or clothes enough; each will, in its own 
way, suggest the other and give a further hint at God, but 
there is no satisfaction inthem. Then, again, creatures being 
mortal will wear out and fall into decay, so there is no pro- 
gressing in them. The longer I gaze at a white sheet or at the 
light of the sun the more tired and dim becomes my sight. 
No creature was ever so noble or so fair but looking at it long 
was wearisome. Spiritual perception, on the other hand, goes 
on increasing without end. The more I know of spiritual 
things the readier and quicker is my mind to take in more.— 
Thirdly, God’s gift to the soul was not made from the vessel 

SERMONS 163 

she flowed out of; no joy, no boon God could bestow, unless it 
came out of the vessel which God is himself, would the soul 
ever relish or take any pleasure in. God is nothing but pure 
being, while creature is from nothing and has gotten being 
from that same divine being, so it stands to reason that the 
soul gets no enjoyment out of creature which is another vessel. 
The draught, however rare and fine, if drunk out of a dirty cup 
will be the baser for it. And that is why, with any gift or 
honour God may give, unless it is given in himself it is of no 
value. - 

According to one authority the Father has a Son and a Holy 
Spirit and through these two he has stooped down to man. 
Also, we know that heaven is immeasurably vaster than this 
earth. The angels in heaven are many more in number than 
all the men on earth. And doctors declare that the angels 
are mostly manifesting God, so he must be greatest of all. 
Daniel saw a thousand thousand serving him and ten hundred 
thousand thousand round about him, while God, seemingly 
forgetting all this lordly train, was giving the whole of his 
attention to a man, to capturing his love. ‘ Woe unto him,’ 
says the Lord, ‘ who does not heed my call.’ This refers, in 
the first place, to God’s calling forth creatures in the creation. 

Secondly, he calls by death ; surely and bitterly it falls upon 
the sinner’s heart. The scriptures say, ‘ Alas, O Death, how 
bitter is the thought of thee to those in pursuit of worldly 
pleasure.’ But to the good it is very sweet and joyful, simply 
the passage from death into life ; for man a very good bargain, 
and the good man does get a rare bargain by dying: he ex- 
changes his pain for eternal delight. Suppose one knew a 
herb was to be had and whoever kept it by him would never 
grow old or fall sick, that herb would be worth its weight in 
gold. And such is death. Always having it in mind prevents 
a man from growing old in sin. Solomon says, * Thinking on 
thy end thou shalt never sin.’ 

In the third place, we shall have our reward, according to 
his words, ‘Summon the labourers and give them their hire.’ 

164 MEISTER ECKHART 

No one need be anxious at our Lord’s ordering the labourers 
to be paid. If a man is sick or old and incapable of bodily 
exertion let him confine himself to inward spiritual works, 
which are better and more important in God’s eyes than out- 
ward actions ; good-will and love to God, to wit, for these he 
is rewarded. God lures us with rewards as sheep which man 
would put elsewhere are lured by tender green. God has 
promised us reward ; he has not said what the reward will be. 
To tell what the reward will be would cost God all his power, 
for all God is and can is the reward. 

I say, if all souls with their powers were one single soul 
she would be unable to receive nor could she bear the due 
reward of the smallest act ordained by God in his eternal 
love: she would be undone and would dissolve and flow 
away. How then can the soul receive it all, all that God is, 
except by being raised above herself and above all creatures 
to a divine condition? In that way she can. Also, the 
reward might stale with age, since it lasts for ever. But God 
has found for this a secret (? heavenly) cure, that of self- 
renewal, and thereby has brought eternity into time and taken 
time into eternity with him: as the Son pours into eternity 
creatures are all poured back in him. The Son is ever being 
born, all at once and all the time, and creaturely desire and 
attainment all gathered to a head in him is presented to the 
soul unceasingly and new. It follows that his birth to-day is 
just as new as when it first began. Of the soul’s reward being 
given thus in a new vessel, fresh and perfect and keeping fresh 
and perfect without end, of this I will say nothing except, may 
it be ours. So help us God. Amen. 

XXXII 

‘Mary stood at the sepulchre weeping.’ In this sermon Meister 
Eckhart tells of Mary Magdalene seeking our Lord and teaches that to 
find him the soul needs six qualifications. 

“ Mary stood at the sepulchre weeping ’ (John 20,,). It was 
a wonder that in such sore distress she could weep at all. 

SERMONS 165 

Love was the reason cf her standing, sorrow of her weeping. 
She stepped forward and looked into the tomb. Seeking one 
dead man she found two living angels. Origen says, ‘ She 
stood. Why did she stay when the apostles had fled?’ She 
had nothing to lose : all she had she had lost with him. When 
he died she died too. When they buried him they buried ber 
soul with him, so she had nothing to lose. 

She went on. Then he met her. She thought he was the 
‘ gardener, and said, ‘Where hast thou laid him?’ In her 
anxiety about him she gave no answer to his question, only, 
‘ Where hast thou laid him?’ Those were her words. Then 
he showed her plainly who he was. Had he declared himself 
at once, while she was in the throes of longing, she would have 
died of joy. If the soul knew when God was coming to her 
she would die of joy; and if she knew when he was leaving 
her she would die of grief. She knows not when he comes:or 
when he goes : well does she know when he is with her. As 
it is said, * His coming and his going are concealed ; his presence 
is no secret, for he is the light and light is by nature revelation.’ 

Mary sought God and God alone; that is why she found 
him : because she wanted God and nothing else. The soul in 
search of God, to her all creatures mean pain.. She was pained 
at seeing the angels: and to the soul in quest of God all things 
must count for naught. |For the soul to find God she will need 
six qualifications: first, what before was sweet to her she 
(now) finds bitter ; next, her soul becomes too cramped for 
her, she is not able to contain herself ; third, she wants nothing 
but God ; fourth, no one but God can console her; fifth, she 
puts no trust in mortal things; sixth, she has no peace of 
mind till he is back with her again, etc. 

XXXIIT 

Here Meister Eckhart’s theme is the state the soul has to be in to receive 
divine light and how God brings forth its twelve fruits in the soul. 

Cum sero factum esset etc (Joh. 20,5). * At eventide, at close 
of day, (when the doors were shut) where the disciples were: 

166 MEISTER ECKHART 

assembled, God came in.’ When the day of earthly pleasures 
is drawing to its close, when the eventide of mortal things 
descends upon the soul, then, her powers being assembled and 
held in, the light of universal truth illuminates the soul. Man 
has to die to sin and to every occasion of sin. Thereafter he 
has to die to nature, for as he is nothing of himself he may 
seek nothing for himself but only God’s glory. Then, being 
God’s own, God can lustily do his own work in the soul. Adam 
was wholly God’s own before he fell; his will was in perfect 
harmony with God’s, so that the divinity, shining through his 
will into his lowest powers, kept them from acting except as 
ordered by the will: God was doing his own work there, 
coming and going as he pleased in that soul. As Christ said, 
“ All power is given to me in heaven and in earth and to Jeru- 
salem,’ or, in other words, ‘I work in the soul that dwells in 
peace, in her I am given the power to do my own work.’ 

That which does its own work will work lustily, as the Holy 
Ghost does in the soul. What does he do? (He brings forth) 
twelve fruits which dispose men to God and good living. The 
‚first three lead men to God: love, which raises a man above 
impermanent things and sets him in God whom he loves, for 
the soul which is wrapped in the fire of true love, her perish- 
able part will be surely burnt away in that amiable fire.—The 
second fruit is spiritual joy, outcome of pure consciousness 
and a great encouragement to virtue generally, since it lifts 
a man above himself. The soul rejoices at its coming.—The 
third is peace of mind, and this makes God dwell in the soul. 

The next three govern a man’s conduct towards his fellow- 
Christian. The first is kindness, wishing with all his heart 
for everyone’s happiness. The second is honesty, treating his 
neighbour as himself. The third is gentleness, behaving to 
people so as not to cause trouble. 

The third three fruits equip a man for future sufferings. The 
first is patience, docility under the burden of suffering ; not 
like a horse which flags before the journey’s end under the load 
it has to carry. The second is long-suffering, not seeking a 

SERMONS 167 

way out of suffering. The third is good temper that no trial 
can embitter or disturb. 

The fourth three fruits pertain to self-control. The first is 
temperance, the second continence, and the third perfection is, 
never ‚taking so much of anything but that one could take 
more, thus desire ever keeps sober. 

XXXIV 

In this discourse Meister Eckhart enumerates and discusses. three 
dignities of our Lady: first that of her birth, next that of her holy life 
on earth, and thirdly the dignity of being God’s mother. 

Que est ista que ascendit quasi aurora consurgens, pulchra ut 
luna, electa ut sol ? (Cant. 6,). In the Book of Love it is written, 
‘ Who is she that rises like the dawn rising, lovely like the 
moon, chosen like the sun?’ Here we note three nobilities 
or dignities of our Lady: first, her birth, referred to when he 
says, ‘ she rises like the dawn rising.’ The second dignity, her 
holy life on earth, he speaks of as ‘ lovely like the moon.’ The 
third, her being God’s mother answers his description, * elect 
(or chosen) like the sun.’ 

The first, likening her to the dawn, suggests to me two facts : 
one, that in this dawn there is both light and darkness; the 
other, that it is the ending of the night and the beginning of the 
day. This latter is true of the birth of our Lady, for it meant 
the end of woe and the birth of joy to those of old, since before 
that time there was nothing they could do to get to heaven. 
But now our Lord is satisfied with very little. He will give 
his heavenly kingdom for a drink of cold water. That, with 
a pure heart, is enough. ‘ Blesséd are the pure in heart,’ Christ 
says. He makes no mention of gross, external, corporal acts. 
And St Bernard cries, ‘ Would to God we longed for Christ as 
much as those of old did for his coming,’ for with mortal things 
there is more pleasure in the thought than in the thing itself, 
but the realization of spiritual things is beyond all expectation. 
To think of that old longing is enough to make one weep. 

The second one goes deeper. It too refers to the birth of our 

168 MEISTER ECKHART 

Lady. That this dawn is made up of both light and darkness 
betokens that our Lady was conceived in sin, her body and her 
soul were united in original sin, and directly afterwards (i.e. after 
animation) she was purified by the Holy Ghost and was born 
holy, and therefore do we celebrate her birth. Herein we are 
shown the perfect(ion) of the body of our Lord, for he never 
made mere creature so noble as that, nor would he make one 
so noble that soul and body were united therein or could be. 
True, God means the soul to see and hear what is not God, but 
he would not have her love anything but him, for he has made 
her for union with himself. He has lavished truth on creature, 
but she is not truth itself. And yet in many ways truth does 
pertain to creatures, in the same sense that God is truth itself, 
thus six is more than two to them, and so on. Did the soul 
find any creature which was truth itself she would stay therein. 
As our Lady says, ‘In all things I have sought rest.” And 
Noé sent forth the dove from the ark, i.e. the rational soul, 
which can in fact find no rest in creatures. So she returns to 
her maker, just like the dove to the ark. In cantico the soul 
is called a dove. By nature the soul loves nothing but good. 
The fact is, every man, if he is really sensible, will find he loves 
nothing but perfect good, and that is why God has not given 
perfect good to any creature. If the soul found perfect good 
in creature she would unite with it. God knows well that love 
is a unifying power: what she wholly loves she unites with, 
and this God does not grudge to any creature, for love takes 
a man out of himself and settles him in what he loves. St Mary 
Magdalene’s soul was united with the dead body of Jesus Christ 
rather than with her own. According to St Augustine, ‘ The 
soul is rather where she loves than where she is giving life.’ 
And St Paul declares, ‘I live yet not I, Christ liveth in me.’ 
All creatures are crying aloud to man, ‘Thou lookest for truth 
and goodness which we are not: seek God, he is both truth 
and goodness.’ Man is always searching for holiness and happi- 
ness. Satisfaction, perfection, does not belong to creature. 
One leads on to another, food to drink and drink to clothes, 

SERMONS 169 

etc. In all these things we are allowed to taste the joys of 
the perfect good in God. St Augustine says, ‘ Are ye seeking, 
then look in God.’ (God) is the end of creature, or the soul 
could never have full satisfaction in God, or peace. In God 
the soul will have all perfection at once; if any of it were 
outside God she would want to have that as well: she would 
forget the most for the least and pay the penalty. St Augustine 
says, ‘ It is arrant nonsense that the soul is without him who 
is everywhere, that she is without him without whom she 
cannot be, that she loves not him apart from whom she cannot 
love at all.’ I say, and it is true, God can no more escape the 
soul than he can escape himself. So far as she can know him 
and is ready to receive him he is obliged to give himself to her, 
aye and to every creature just as much as each can take of him 
and is his likeness. Here I stand, and if a number of mirrors 
were held in front of me the reflection of my face would be 
cast therein; I could no more avoid it than I could avoid 
myself. The clearer the mirror the better the likeness. Thus 
one can always recognize his presence ina creature. ‘Old new 
good,’ cries St Augustine, ‘ why have I been so long in finding 
thee! Old, for thou art eternal; new, for thou art ever full 
of life.’ Also, he says, does St Augustine, ‘I was seeking thee 
and found myself away from thee in a land of unlikeness : 
not very far, for thou art everywhere, and not that I was hid 
from thee since all things are within thy ken, but I was shrouded 
in unlikeness and failed to recognize thee.’ 

So much for the first point : our Lady arose ‘ like the dawn.’ 
The second is, ‘fair as the moon.’ In two ways our Lady 
resembles the moon, the lowest of the planets and the smallest 
all but one which is smaller still and stands for Christ who was 
most abject in humility. Next to him comes Mary. St 
Bernard says, ‘ The chastity of our Lady was acceptable to 
God, but humility made her God’s mother.’ The other way 
is (this): the moon gives more light than other stars because 
it is lower than the other planets are, and this suggests the perfect 
compassion of our Lady, for with a waxing moon creatures come 

170 MEISTER ECKHART 

more alive and stronger than those conceived when the moon 
ison the wane. Then as to earth. This is the least of all the 
elements as well as being the basest. Each one of them is ten 
thousand times greater than the other: water and air and fire. 
The earth floats in mid-heaven because all the stellar power 
which runs into the other eıements goes through them, whereas 
upon the earth alone the powers of all the different stars can 
meet, because of the fixity of earth! which does not circulate 
like the other elements. That is why the power of light pro- 
duces such incredible effects on earth. So with our Lady: 
every perfection God could possibly give creature she has 
received. 

By the same token we should know what persons our Lord 
is with, We may know it by two signs, compassion and 
genuine humility ; but many a man fancies himself humble 
who is far from being so, while one who weens himself the 
basest, the vilest among creatures receives all good and perfect 
things wrought by our Lord in creatures. The other sign is 
compassion which is shown ... .? if someone else has eyeache 
it matters not to me, for it is not my eye; only so far as Iam 
one with him does it affect me too. But supposing I am God’s 
rather than my own, then if my eye is painful it will not pain 
me. Why? Because it is not mine. And so with all my 
worries, they will not worry me since I am not my own. St 
Augustine says, ‘Our Lord behaves as if he took more pleasure 
in our happiness than we do ourselves and felt more SOrrow 
for our pain.’ 

The third dignity of our Lady he expresses in the words 
* chosen like the sun.’ The sun is a light bearer; it is not the 
light itself, it only contains it and pours it into all creatures, 
and no creature is born or completed without the help of its 
light. It does not shine by night, but pours its light into the 
stars. It is called the chief light-bearer because of its profound 
effect on precious stones as well as many other things upon 

1 It was not till 1530 that Copernicus proved in his De Revolutionibus 
that the earth travels round the sun. 2 MS. illegible. 

SERMONS 171 

the earth; hence the virtues that we find in stones. And she 

is a light-bearer, for she brought us the true light into the 
world. We beseech our Lord, etc. 

XXXV 

Here Meister Eckhart tells of two kinds of suffering. By one kind here 
in time we earn reward. In the other we receive our reward in heaven, 

in the passive intellect. 

Non sunt condigne passiones huius temporis ad futuram gloriam 
que revelabitur in nobis (Rom. 8,,). ‘The sufferings of this 
present time are not worthy to compare with the glory which 
shall be revealed in us.’ St Augustine comments, ‘ they are 
unworthy,’ as though one said to another, ‘ what you say is 
not true’; if he said, ‘ you say truly,’ that would be confirma- 
tion. They are not worthy. The apostles left the concilio 
rejoicing that they were worthy to suffer for God’s sake. The 
good and virtuous man would rejoice to merit any suffering 
for God. To anyone who has a lively sense of God and is 
consumed with the fire of love the world appears not worth 
a straw. St John says in Apocalypse, ‘Sell all thou hast and 
buy fiery gold,’ that is, love, for he who has this has all things. 
The right good man, man as he is by rights, how readily, how 
gladly he will suffer pain, purgatory, anything for God. This 
none can see, but he who has it knows. ‘I thirst,’ Christ said, 
at the end of all his sufferings. He meant that he was ready 
to suffer more still for mankind. 

So much for the sufferings of this world: there is suffering 
of another world. Our life is divided into two, the one is 
working and the other suffering. Working we earn our wages, 
suffering we receive them. No one knows the trouble that 
God takes to draw the soul. Our earnings come from working, 
but that is poor and meagre, so he pays not by the work but 
by the suffering. Always our best he goes by, for we can do 
but little and suffer a great deal, give little and take much. 
- A man can take a pound who cannot give a penny. And 
much he takes more readily than little: the bigger and better 

172 MEISTER ECKHART 

the thing the more gladly it is received. By rewarding us 
according to our suffering he can give us a great deal since we 
are liable to have so much. Suffering is destitute, doing has 
something. I can do nothing I have not got, unless it is in 
me, but suffering has nothing, it is bare. A philosopher says, 
“ Where one is made out of twain, one must needs go out: 
of itself, must die in itself, that it may be changed into its 
fellow and be one therewith.’ Before a sense can perceive it 
has to be free from perceptions: this eye must be without 
colour before it can recognize colour, and the ear free from 
sound before it can hear, and so on with each of the senses ; 
and so far as a sense goes out of itself so far is it able to take 
in more and unite with what it takes in. In the same way the 
soul must have done with herself before she can take God in, 
then she becomes united with God and does all his godly work 
with him. This was the reward Christ craved when, after all 
his travail and his sufferings, he cried, ‘ Father, I pray thee 
that they may be one as we are one.’ Nor is God satisfied 
with suffering only. The recompense being sure God makes 
it easy for us, permitting that we acquire merit and obtain 
reward by willingly and gladly bearing hardship in his 
name. 

The sufferings of this time are not worthy, they are un- 
worthy to compare with the glory which shall be discovered 
in us. ‘Discovered.’ If it is discovered in us it must be 
there already. All the powers which overlay, the soul will 
have to fall away if God is to be found and made manifest in 
us. When God created the soul he planted himself in her and 
was covered over: this is the vineyard of God wherein he 
himself is the plant. Directly he made this nature, even 
before he had made her, he was ready to plant himself 
therein. 

No man can see God. St Philip said, ‘ Show us the Father 
and it sufficeth us.” We hymn our Lady, ‘ Show thyself, that 
thou art mother; if thou art God’s mother, show us, for so 
doing thou mayst help us; show us that thou art our mother, 

SERMONS 173 

for as our mother thou wilt help us ; show thyself mother since 
thou hast a child,’ for they go together. A mother must have 
a child. ‘Show us the Father.’ And a father must have a 
child. These are dependent on each other: there is no having 
one without the other. He who knows the Father knows the 
Son, since all that is gotten in the Father is begotten in the 
Son. To know him we must be cnildren : being children we 
are of course heirs. 

Happiness lies in knowing God, not from the outside as we 
look at things: what we see outside us in separation, none of 
that is God. God-consciousness is life streaming out of the 
essence of God and the soul, for God and the soul have one 
being, are one in essence, and all activities flow out of God so’ 
that they still remain in him. The soul knows God where she 
is one in and with the essence of God. True happiness this, 
having life and being with God, and God-knowledge is that 
wherein all other knowing and being has faded away. The 
soul is not aware of herself or other things, only of herself in 
God and God in her, all things in him ; everything in God she 
knows with him, and with him she is doing all his works. There 
is nothing, she knows nothing, but herself in God and God 
in her 

The very first power to spring up in the bare ground is 
pure perception. Issuing bare it is instantly clothed; covered 
up; yet therein it falls on pure being. Straightway it draws 
a sheath round itself, truth, to wit. This recognizes the 
true. But will will have nothing save what makes for ...1! 
good or seems good. 

He says, ‘ which shall be discovered in us.’ ‘In us.’ The 
word ‘us’ stands for pure being. For us to reach the point 
at which this glory is revealed in us the soul must be divested 
of hope, fear, sadness, pleasure—all contingent things. Then 
God in turn reveals himself to her and gives himself to her with 
all he can.—Next, we must look within, not without, for, says 
St Paul, ‘The Kingdom of God is within you.—Thirdly, in the 

1 MS. illegible. 

174 MEISTER ECKHART 

very innermost where he is somewhat unveiled, there per- 
adventure we may see him face to face. So help us, etc. 

XXXVI! 
Here the venerable Meister Eckhart talks of two kinds of wisdom and 

two kinds of happiness which result therefrom, i.e. from divine wisdom 
and from temporal wisdom. 

Beatus homo qui invenit sapientiam (Prov. 8,3). There are 
two kinds of wisdom, and two kinds of happiness which come 
from wisdom. One wisdom is temporal, where man takes 
himself as a creature of time and can adapt himself to it: the 
art, as it were, of avoiding misfortune and profiting by the 
occasion. Anybody good at this becomes a wealthy man and 
is said to be blest with worldly wisdom. But worldly wisdom 
is a bar to eternal wisdom. Our God has given the soul two 
sets of powers so that she may serve him with the lower powers 
in time and with the higher ones in eternity. One philosopher 
says, ‘The soul is a point, a corner, where time and eternity 
collide ; not that the soul is made of eternity or time, she is a 
nature made from naught between them both.’ If she were 
made of time she would be mortal, if of eternity she would be 
unchangeable. Augustine (says), ‘ The soul is descended from 
a most noble heavenly naught ; better our whole life were 
given to the search than that we (n)ever find it.” The soul 
must be noble to withstand the assaults of voth eternity 
and time. Yielding to temporal things she becomes un- 
stable; holding to eternity she grows strong and steady, 
and by fortitude and steadiness she triumphs over changing 
things. 

The other wisdom is eternal, and this wisdom is a well-spring 
of divine illumination, a fount of divine truth, and in this 
wisdom man finds eternal happiness. One who is minded to 
attain this wisdom will need humility and industry and a 
penetrating passivity. No activity is so perfect but it hinders 
recollection. The hearing of the mass permits of recollection 

! See also Jostes, No. 73, which is rather fuller. 

SERMONS © 175 

more than the saying of it does. A celebrant over-much 
intent on recollection is liable to make mistakes. The best 
way is to try and concentrate the mind before and afterwards, 
but when actually saying it to do so quite straightforwardly. 
And poverty too goes with divine wisdom. Poor to some 
purpose is the man who beggars himself of all the things which 
are not God. 

Mark next which people it is that divine wisdom comes to. 
Solomon says it is to ‘ her who opens her mouth to wisdom.’ 
The mouth means the highest power of the soul, where the 
soul is laved by God. This power ought always to be directed 
upwards and opened to godly consolation, and what it gets 
from God it pours into the lower powers. If she could grasp 
or take in God with her lower powers God would be demeaned, 
brought down to our understanding; we are not able to lay 
hold of God with anything pertaining to this life. One philo- 
sopher says, ‘ Whatever we can say of God God is’; another 
one declares ‘ God is nothing that we can express,’ and both of 
them are right. According to Augustine, ‘God is power, 
wisdom and goodness,’ (whereas) Dionysius says, ‘God is 
above wisdom and above goodness; he is above anything we 
‚can express.” In the Scriptures God is given many names, and 
for this there are two reasons : one, because his majesty cannot 
be expressed in any words at all, he is above and beyond nature, 
possessing as he does an unnatured nature. Sometimes they 
say he is a power at other times a light. He transcends all 
light. They call him this or that because he is not really any 
of these things. If his grandeur could be realized by dint of 
any words, these names would be carefully concealed. He 
who knows God best denies most to him, as we can see with a 
ship. Suppose I wanted to describe a ship to someone who 
had never seen one and I said, it is not like a stone nor like a 
blade of grass. At once I should have told him something 
about the ship. Two learned men were at their prayers. One 
besought our Lord by his power and his wisdom, the other one 
said, ‘ Hush, thou dost dishonour God. God is far above aught 

176 MEISTER ECKHART 

that we can say ; were God not so lenient and had the saints 
not given and God accepted it from them I would never dare 
to give him praise in words.’ From this reverent knowledge 
a reverent fear is gotten in the soul, and in this fear God is 
sown in the soul and the soul dies in God. As our Lord says, 
* Except a corn of wheat fall into the ground and die it brings 
forth no fruit.” This death the soul dies in the intuition of 
God, which she feels in herself and which makes to stink in her 
nostrils all things which are not God: God so pours himself 
out to her in grace and takes root in faith, abiding in love. 
St N(icolaus) has proved how good, how excellent, a bargain 
it is to give up all for wisdom. St Elizabeth joyfully resigned 
her kingdom to be poor. It is recorded of her that, ‘ Her light 
did not go out at night,’ meaning that in times of trouble she 
could always see her way. So shall her light shine in eternal — 
life. However perfect a man is, the loss of any temporal 
possession will trouble and upset him. There is no doubt that 
anyone who suffers loss against his will and bears it patiently 
merits more reward than would be his due for giving willingly 
to our Lord God, for so he would be doing his own will. But 
by resignation he is giving both his will and his goods to God. 
If anyone is found impatient of misfortune his naughtiness 
appears as pain, the man behaving like any copper penny: 
while not lying in the fire it has a silver sheen, but once it gets 
into the fire it shows that it was copper. God has tried the 
saints by pain till, perfect in all the virtues, they shine here 
in the might and in eternal life for ever. _ 

The third (thing) is how to taste divine eternal wisdom. 
Four things make for this. The first is likeness, making our- 
selves like God in clearness, as glass and transparent things 
are to the sun. The second thing is divine light which shines 
through the clear soul like the sun through glass or water. 
The third is union, this comes from likeness, as light from light. 

The fourth is measure, God being commensurate to the soul. 
But God cannot be diminished or increased, for he is measure- 
less and motionless. The soul then must be lifted and 

SERMONS 177 

enlarged, for she is small and moveable; she has to be raised 

above herself and widened somewhat for God’s infinitude. We 
pray, etc. 

XXXVII 

Meister Eckhart tells in this sermon how an angel shows himself to man 
and the soul and what state the soul has to be in if grace is to work there. 

‚Elizabeth pariet tibi filium et vocabis nomen eius Johannem 
(Luc. 1,3). ‘Elizabeth shall bear a son, and he shall be called 
John.’ This was said by the angel when he appeared to 
Zacharias. An angel can appear in two ways, (first) in bodily 
guise, this he gets from the elements. 

An angel can normally cause to appear within the hour a 
tree which would take years to grow from seed. Human 
nature changes food into flesh and blood, much more then is 
it possible that by the power of God bread and wine should 
be changed into God’s body.—Secondly, an angel manifests 
himself in the likeness of divine light to make God’s will known 
to the soul, and by charging the light with God’s will he im- 
presses it upon the soul. Just as the words I am about to 
speak are not the thing I want to speak about, but I with 
my voice commit my words to the air and the air conveys 
them to your ears and so they reach your soul. There are 
some religious who show with their fingers what they mean. 
Similarly angels show themselves in incorporeal likeness and 
so make God’s will known to the soul. 

Take now the first words spoken by the angel, ‘ Elizabeth 
shall bear a child.” From Elizabeth we know the condition of 
the soul wherein God’s grace is born. John means grace- 
containing. ‘The child shall be great, born full of grace.’ 
Three sorts of births are pure from the mother’s womb. St 
John was (born) so pure that he could do no mortal sin; our 
Lady too was full of grace, she never committed any sin at all, 
mortal or venial; and our Lord Jesus Christ was absolutely 
pure, gotten as he was of one so purified for his conception 

that he was never subject to original sin. Just as the Virgin 
12 

178 MEISTER ECKHART 

was detached so that soul must be in whom God’s grace is to 
be born, not occupied with pleasant and unpleasant thoughts 
of people but intent on God alone. — 

Activity flows from essence. If there were no being there 
would be no doing. Heat comes from fire: no fire no heat. 
Cold comes from earth, and all very earthy things are dull- 
witted and sluggish and cold. Perfection of soul then depends 
upon heat which stirs it to lively activity. . 

By three things we know there is grace in the soul. First, 
by her being like God, for she is descended from divinity. 
Secondly, by her behaving like God, God’s likeness stamped 
in the soul making her so much like God that to the devils she 
is presented as God, so noble the nature of grace. Thirdly, by 
the fact that the soul is not content unless it has all perfection 
at once, for as the heathen philosopher: says, ‘ Perfection of 
soul means having likeness to God’s angel as well as to all 
creatures.’ Likeness and all creaturely perfection is created 
in the angels mentally before it is produced in creatures. Now 
the soul shall be like the angels in heaven ; what the angels 
have possessed has been promised to the soul ; to her shall be 
given what the angels have received. So the soul is never 
satisfied until she gets to where creaturely perfection is all one 
and undivided. 

Next let us see how grace operates in the soul. We may 
compare it with an axe. This requires three things : first it 
must be well trimmed and polished. And so must the soul . 
be stripped clean of sins, not able to sin, for the man of sin 
cannot do right without grace, without divine likeness ; how- 
ever much good he may do it will profit him nothing. —This 
axe, again, brings about the workman’s desired end. Even so 
grace brings the soul into God; it transports the soul over 
herself and robs her of self and of all that is creature and unites 
her with God. Grace works with the soul till she herself has 
‘ to make room, and then nothing is left there but God and the 

soul. We pray, etc. 

SERMONS. | 179 

XXXVITI 

Here Meister Eckhart teaches that we must overcome four kingdoms, 
the kingdom of our Lord Jesus Christ being won by the power of love. 

Sancti per. fidem vicerunt regna (Hebr. 1153). St Paul says, 
‘ By faith the saints subdued kingdoms,’ Four kingdoms have 
the saints subdued and we too must subdue them. The first 
is the world. The earthly kingdom is subdued by spiritual 
poverty. The next kingdom, of the flesh, we conquer by hunger 
and by thirst. The third is of the devil, and this we overcome 
by sorrow and by pain. The fourth one is the kingdom of 
our Lord Jesus Christ, and this is won by the power of love. 

What though a man have all the world yet must he look 
upon himself as poor and all the time be reaching out his hand 
before the door of our Lord God, asking for the grace of God, 
soliciting his alms, for. grace makes men God’s children. As 
David cries, ‘ Lord, all my desire is of thee and for thee.’ And 
St Paul says, ‘I have counted all things as dung that I may 
win Christ.’ It-is impossible for any soul to be free from sin 
without the protection of God’s grace. The function of grace 
is to quicken the soul and fit her for all divine uses, for grace 
flows out in a divine stream which savours of God and is a 
likeness of God and makes the soul Godlike. When grace with 
its savour is warped into the will it is called love and when 
this grace with its savour is warped into the intellect it is called 
the light of faith, and this same grace and savour warped into 
the irascible power goes by the name of hope. These divine 
virtues are so-called because they do divine work in the soul, 
just as we see with the light of the sun its energizing action 
on the earth, giving life to everything and keeping it in being. 
If this light goes, everything goes, as though it was not. And 
so with the soul. Where grace is and love things are easy to 
do. It is a sure sign if a man finds good works hard to do 
that there is no grace in him. A wise man says, “I do not 
judge of people by the fine clothes they wear or the good food 
they eat, but by whether they have love. I feel none the 

180 MEISTER ECKHART 

better for. leading a hard life but only if I find I have more 
love.’ Much prayer and fasting, strenuous work and so forth 
is the greatest folly if a man does not reform his ways but is _ 
irritable and restless. It behoves him to take stock, that 
man, of his morbid state and of all his faults, and turn his whole 
attention to-overcoming them. When a man leads a well- 
regulated life then whatever he does will please God, and so 
he conquers the kingdoms. rogemus. 

XXXIX 

Here Meister Eckhart explains in what way the soul brings forth fruit, 
who abideth in God and in whom God abideth. 

Qui manet in me et ego etc (Joh. 6,,). Christ says, ‘he that 
abideth in me and I in him the same bringeth forth much 
fruit.’ This saying is divided into three. First, ‘he that 
abideth or dwelleth in me’; next, ‘and I in him’; and 
thirdly, ‘he shall bring forth much. fruit.’ With regard to 
the first St Paul says, ‘ Your life is hid with Christ in God’: 
inasmuch as ye are dead with him and risen with him your life 
is hid with Christ in the heavenly Father. Now let us see 
what people are dwelling thus in God. One sign of them is that 
they are warm ; there is no half-heartedness, tardiness or dis- 
inclination in them to good works. We have ,.. that the 
waters are not frozen at their source: that is because the sun 
draws the moisture from the bottom of the hill up to the top 
and out of the hill so that it flows. It is the heat that does it, 
that makes it warm and living at the source, and the further 
it flows the colder and more impure it gets. It is the same 
with man. The further off he is from God the more stagnant 
and cold and insipid he will be. In a book about the curse 
of worldly pleasures and the pursuit of them St Ambrose lays 
it down that we must look for God with God. Man is low 
and God is high. Man therefore has to hoist himself up high 
above the world by means of divine love: love will break into 
God and pilot the soul about God and make everything clear 
to her in God. So far as the soul can understand and grasp 

SERMONS 181 

it, the.power of love will raise her up to the highest that there 
is in God: to goodness namely, and in company with good- 
ness she will flow out with God in all divine activities, in the 
goodness wherein God created heaven and earth. Our Lord says, 
“ Who can find a faithful and wise servant, one so faithful that 
he seeks no advantage for himself but God’s glory alone?’ Any 
servant who continues humbly in his service, to him I make this 
solemn promise: all God is and can, that verily he shall receive. 

The second thing our Lord says is: ‘ He that abideth in me 
and I in him.’ According to St Bernard it is far better that 
God should be in us than that we should be in God. For God 
to establish himself in us, for him to be living and moving in 
us, means that the soul is shaping her life to God, to the change- 
less eternal nature of God, and is actively living the life of God 
in what measure he metes it out to her. To which I add this 
third consideration, that since God is the life and being of the 
soul she is not sensible of aught save God in her motives and 
her acts. One unfailing sign that God is dwelling thus within 
the soul is that she is at rest. In all his works God seeks 
nothing but rest, and the soul is able to please him by nothing 
so much as by rest. The soul is never so like him as she is 
when at rest, keeping still. 

Thirdly, our Lord says, ‘He that abideth in me and I in him 
the same shall bring forth much fruit.’ If I wanted to tell 
what these fruits are I should not know how to: I could not, 
I know that quite well. It has been said that * he who knows 
God well enough to know he is unknowable to creature knows 
God most particularly, and he who plainly sees that no man 
can know God knows God most perfectly.’ The fruits which 
God has promised can be known only by certain signs. We 
note six signs of them: first, the soul weakens in her sins, like 
an old building which gives way when it is shaken. This is 
a sign it will be built anew if the man who pulls it down can 
do the work or face the cost. Even so the soul succumbs to 
God and devotes all her powers to his works.—The second sign 
is the appearance of true virtues in the soul where steadfast- 

182 MEISTER ECKHART 

ness, trustworthiness and freedom follow upon consciousness. 
— The third sign is that divine things firmly strike root in the 
heart, and the man finds that things have no relish, give him 
no pleasure, unless they are godly.—The fourth sign is that 
what has been withered by sins and shortcomings all grows 
supple again, verdant and vital and vigorous with grace.—The 
fifth sign is that all the powers of the soul become enlightened, 
no speck of sin or ignorance is left, the soul is all one light. 
But divine light enters the soul not openly by the door but 
covertly and veiled so that the soul is hardly conscious of when 
God comes and goes, and this God does in kindness, this keep- 
ing himself dark and well concealed. Suppose God came openly 
into the soul she would not be able to bear it, she would be 
discomfited and all undone with love and joy. And if God 
left her openly she could not bear that either, but would be 
worse discomfited and distraught the more, for divine light 
and bliss are overwhelming, nor can the soul endure these 
twain untempered any better than the eye can stand the sun- 
light untempered by the air.—The sixth sign is that what in 
the body and the soul has been destroyed and frozen is gathered 
up into God and suddenly inflamed with divine love. We 
pray, etc. 
XL 

Here Meister Eckhart explains that the soul must transcend her own 
nature if she would receive the teaching of God and behold the high nature 
of God and taste the sweetness of God. » 

Dimissa turba ascendit in montem (Matt. 14.5). The evangelist 
says, ‘Our Lord dismissed the crowd and ascending into the 
mountain he opened his mouth and taught about the kingdom 
of God.’ ‘God taught.’ Augustine (says), ‘He who teaches 
there has his chair in heaven.” He who would receive God’s 
teaching must ascend and transcend all creatures and renounce 
them. To receive divine teaching a man has to gather himself 
together and shut himself up in himself away from cares and 
worries and the coil of nether things. The soul has powers, 
many of them, very much divided; these he has to overcome, 

SERMONS 188 

even the ones which are in the mind. Thought which indeed 
works wonders when it is in itself, this must be mastered if God 
is to speak in the powers which are impartible. 

Again, he ascends into the mountain, i.e. God reveals the 
sublimity and sweetness of his nature where all that is evil 
must needs fall away. There the soul knows nothing but God 
and herself and is in the guise of God’s image. 

Thirdly, by ascending he symbolized his lofty nature. That 
which is high is close to God, those soul powers for example 
which are so near to God. Our Lord on one occasion took 
three of his disciples and leading them up into a mountain 
appeared to them (in) the same glorious body that we shall 
have in eternal life. Our Lord said, ‘ Remember, when I spoke 
to you ye saw neither image nor likeness.’ ’ 

St Augustine speaks of three kinds of knowledge. The first 
is corporal, form-seizing, like [that of the] eye which sees and 
receives images. The second is mental, but still aware of forms 
of corporal things. The third knowledge is interior, as it is in 
the spirit, that knows without form and likeness, and that 
knowledge is like unto the angels the host whereof is divided 
into three. According to one authority, ‘The soul does not 
know herself apart from likeness, whereas an angel does know 
himself without likeness, and God.’ Meaning to say, ‘God is in 
the summit of the soul without image and likeness.’ 

‘ He ascended into the mountain and was transformed before 
them.’ The soul shall be transformed, sealed in the form of 
God’s Son. The soul is made in the image of God. The Son 
is the image of God. Where the Son starts the soul starts too, 
at the same point whence he is proceeding. Yet the Son as 
proceeding from the Father the soul is not suspended in, she 
is above form. Fire and heat are one yet far from the same. 
Taste and colour in an apple are one yet far from the same: 
the mouth tastes things which are lost on the eye. The eye 
sees colours unperceived by the tongue. The eye demands 
light, whereas taste holds good in the dark. The soul demands 
light, the soul knows of nothing but one, she is above form. 

184 MEISTER ECKHART 

The prophet says, ‘God will lead his sheep into green pastures.’ 
According to one philosopher, ‘ The celestial flow is nowhere 
so well seen as in the artless brutes. These receive the heavenly . 
influx simply, but people who are wise and have many senses 
are carried away into multitudinous things.’ Our Lord pro- 
mised to pasture his sheep upon the mountain, on young grass. 
Creatures all grow young in God. Creatures all fall out of God 
to start with and then through the angels. That which has 
no special creature nature bears the general stamp of creature. 
That which can take on angelic nature has it in him altogether. 
What God means to create the angel bears within himself, so 
that it may not be deprived of the perfection which other 
creatures have. Why is this? Because of the angel’s near- 
ness to God. Augustine says, ‘What God creates flows out 
through the angels.’ On high everything is young. On that 
mountain-top things are all fresh and green: falling into time 
they grow old and fade. There will our Lord pasture his 
sheep, on the green of all creatures, which exist at that height 
as they are in the angels. The soul would like this green more 
than anything in the world. As different as day from night is 
the least of creatures, there from any mundane thing. He who 
would receive God’s teaching must ascend into his mountain. 
There God will perfect him, in the day of eternity, where the 
light is at the full. What I know in God, that is light. What 
concerns creature is darkness. Perception is light. John 
(says): “ God is the true light, which shines in the darkness.’ 
What is darkness? Clinging, holding on nowhere, being blind 
and unconscious of creature. To see God a man must be blind, 
like St Paul: when he saw into himself he saw God. We 
pray, etc. 
XLI 

In this discourse Meister Eckhart teaches that the soul has to die to 
herself and to all creatures if she is to be receptive to divine being and 
divine life. 

Nisi granum frumenti cadens in terram mortuum fuerit, ipsum 
solum manet (Joh. 12,4). They say this grain of wheat dies 

SERMONS 185 

utterly, losing its shape, its colour and its being. Wheat- 
nature is equally stone-nature then. Receptivity alone re- 
mains. Even so the soul must die if she‘is to grow receptive 
to another nature. Thou must be as the dead towards all 
happenings. God will never otherwise be thy entire being. 
He may give thee various gifts and light and eomfort, much 
prized and very valuable things, but God will never give him- 
self to thee without reserve till thou dost give thyself up 
altogether. As the soul dies in herself God comes to be her 
whole life, and there will remain no more than one, as my body 
and my soul are no more than one.. ‘ He that hateth his soul 
shall keep it,’ says our Lord. The word soul does not stand 
for the nature of the soul: we can no more find a name for the 
nature of the soul than we can for God. Nor does the soul 
grow old, but in her outlook on the body and regard for it we 
may well hate her, for in this respect she is hateful. Thus in 
cantico the soul exclaims, ‘ O ye daughters of Jerusalem regard 
me not as being brown: it is due to two things, to the sun 
having altered my colour and to my mother’s sons fighting 
against me.’ For all temporal things, everything under the 
sun, I have had inordinate affection. It is my mother’s sons 
then who are fighting against me, not my father’s, who are 
well disposed. ‘Call me not brown. I am fair and noble in 
my nature and most gracious withal.’ He that hateth his 
soul in this world shall preserve it. Augustine speaks of two 
worlds. He calls the soul a world, and in each of the angels 
there is a mental world, everything God ever made being 
present in each angel in much nobler form than it is in itself, 
since it is in him immaterially. The truth of it is there, for 
in the angels truth exists albeit not so much as a needle-point 
thereof compared with the ultimate reality. 

Our Lord says, ‘ He that would serve me let him follow me.’ 
Nothing more about following. Some people follow as long as 
things go well with them. It may be objected: our Lord 
demands so much. All he demands is that he may be allowed 
to give himself to us and as the love so is the gift. But since 

186 MEISTER ECKHART 

God is love therefore he gives himself altogether. There is in 
the soul a natural likeness to this, for she exists in every limb 
and in each one whole. At her creation the soul is thus en- 
dowed in the ground of her being. So she can work in her 
members as a whole and in each one severally. 

Now it may be questioned : What does our Lord mean by 
saying, ‘ Where I am there shall also my servant be?’ Perhaps 
he means that God intends to steal from God something he 
wants to give the soul. In the first leaping forth when the 
Son glances out of the Father while at the same time remaining 
within, ere he, the Son, is born, he is imparting himself to her 
therein abiding, where he has no outlook at all, where she 
with him is the Son. Birth is tantamount to becoming. In 
the eternal birth is her becoming ; there she becomes so wholly 
one that she has no other being than the selfsame being which 
is his, to wit, the being of the soul. This being is the source 
of all works wrought by God in heaven and earth. It is the 
ground and origin of all divine activity. In dying to her own 
nature and her being and her life the soul is born in her divinity. 
That is her becoming. She becomes so wholly one that there 
is no distinction except that he stays God and she stays soul. 
We pray, etc. 

XLII 

Puella surge. Taking this as his text Meister Eckhart exhorts the soul 
to arise and turn with her powers to God for the sake of four noble things 
which she finds in God. 

Puella surge (Luc. 8,,). In that one word God tells the soul 
to arise from mortal things, and as the Son is the Word of the 
Father so he teaches in one Word how the soul is to rise, to 
hoist herself up above herself and dwell above her own self. 
The Father spoke one Word, that was his Son. In that single 
Word he uttered all things. Why did he say no more than 
one word? Because all things are present in his mind. If I 
could grasp in one idea all the thoughts I ever had or ever 
shall have then I should have one word, no more, for the 
mouth utters what is in the heart. But no more of this. 

SERMONS 187 

The soul should dwell above herself and for four reasons. 
First, for the manifold delights she finds in God. God’s full- 
ness is not able to contain itself, he must let creatures over- 
flow from him, creatures to whom he can impart himself, who 
can receive his likeness, are poured forth without measure, 
enough, ’twould seem, to empty him: angels more in number 
than seeds or leaves or grasses. Through them all light flows 
to us and gifts and grace. What flows through this nature 
God offers the soul, and it would be as naught if he gave not 

himself in the gift. 
_ Secondly, the soul ascends for the light she finds in God. 
All creatures are light in God. So sure as they flow out of 
God (they) are to God as naught to aught. In God is light 
and being, that is our darkness: naught, which in God is 
light, that is darkness. 

Thirdly, the soul ascends for the same she finds in God, for 
there there is no different. Wisdom and goodness are the same 
in God. What wisdom is that very same is goodness and the 
rest. If in him wisdom were one thing and goodness were 
another there would be no satisfaction for the soul in God, 
but the soul has a natural inclination to God, and creatures 
all have an innate longing for wisdom. Take a soul overflowing 
with goodness, if goodness were one thing and wisdom another 
that soul would with pain have to give up her goodness if she 
wanted to pour forth in wisdom. St Augustine said, ‘ The 
souls which are in heaven are not wholly blest while they still 
have any leaning towards body.’ The soul is not satisfied 
with anything but God, for in God she finds good as a whole. 
The soul must dwell above herself if she is to get God, for 
things all re-produce themselves, each one its own nature. Why 
does the apple-tree nature not produce wine et e contrario ? 
Because that is not its nature. Fire begets fire. Could it 
' grasp its whole content it would beget that. Creature so loves 
its own being that were all the pain that ever was suddenly 
to fall upon the soul she would still refuse to give up her being 
- which she has received direct from God. The soul must dwell 

188 MEISTER ECKHART 

above herself if she means to lay hold of God; however hard 
she may try with the power whereby she grasps created things 
—and all of these she may well grasp by dint of that same power, 
aye, though God made a thousand earths and a thousand 
heavens, with that same power she could grasp them all—yet 
she cannot grasp God. The infinite God who is in the soul, 
he grasps the God who is infinite. Then God conceives 
God, God is gotten by God in the soul, making her like 
him. 

Fourthly, the soul ascends for the limitlessness she finds in 
God, all things in God being free from space and time. (In 
the) Apocalypse (it says), ‘ Behold, I make all things new,’ in 
the eternal birth, that is, and he is being born to-day, he 
flowing into the soul and she flowing back into God, and just 
as one may die of fright so may one die of joy. Thus the soul 
dies to herself ere she ascends into God. She takes four steps 
into God. At the first step she weakens in hope and desire 
and fear. At the next step hope, fear and desire vanish 
completely. The third step makes her all unmindful of them. 
The fourth step means oblivion, wherein she never thinks of 
them again. 

XLITI 

Puella surge. On these words Meister Eckhart teaches how God with 
his Spirit changes into himself the spiritual nature of the soul, giving 
analogies for this and pointing out the things that help and hinder it. 

Puella surge (Luc. 8;,). ‘Arise!’ Our Lord laid his hand 
‘upon the maid and said, ‘ Arise!’ The hand of God, that is 
the Holy Spirit. All things are wrought in heat. When the 
fiery love to God grows cold in the soul she dies, and if God 
is to work in the soul God must be atoned in the soul. For the 
soul to be one with God or to become so, she must be divorced 
from things, she must be all alone as God is all alone, and 
work wrought by God in the lone soul is better than heaven 
and earth. God has made the soul for union with himself. 
Says one of the saints, ‘ The soul was made from naught and 

SERMONS 189 

He alone did make her, no one with him.’ If anyone had 
helped him he would have had the more to fear, lest the soul 
turn that way. The soul must be by herself just as God is 
by himself. Spiritual things and corporal are incompatible. 
If divine perfection is to operate in the soul the soul must be 
spirit even as God is spirit, and if God gives to the soul in the — 
soul he can give only in a limited way. So he takes her into 
himself in himself: thus she becomes one with him. For 
example, when fire and stone come together, both of the 
natural kind, the stone remains quite cold inside owing to the 
denseness of the stone, and similarly with the air and light. 
What thou seest in the air thou seest in the sun. And since 
both of them are natural there is more light in a mile than in 
& half and more in a half than in a house. The best example
