to be found ıs that of the body and the soul. These are so 
closely knit that body is powerless to act apart from soul and 
soul apart from body, and as the soul sticks to the body so 
God sticks to the soul. When the soul does leave the body 
that means the death of the body. And the soul dies if God 
leaves her. 

There are three hindrances to union of the soul with God. 
First, her being too much divided, not simple (pure) enough. 
The soul is not simple in her relations with creatures. The 
second one is attachment to temporal things. And thirdly, 
being fond of the body will prevent union with God. 

Also there are in the soul three aids to union with God. 
First, the soul being one and undivided: to be atoned with 
God she has to be as pure as God is. Next, her being above 
herself and all temporal things and keeping hold of God. 
Thirdly, detachment from all mortal things and perfect freedom 
of action. Concerning the free soul St Augustine says, ‘ When 
thou dost not want (me) I want thee. When I want thee thou 
wilt have none of me. When I pursue thee thou dost flee from 
me.’ Returning, pure souls go running back to the pure nature 
of God. 

190 MEISTER ECKHART 

XLIV! 

Here Meister Eckhart speaks about divine lordship and what it consists 
in and how the sovran good is ordered to the soul and how the soul receives 
its influx and how the soul loves and knows with it. 

Domine rex omnipotens in dicione ‘tua cuncta sunt posita 
(Esth. 18,). Power, lordship, lies in two things, in freedom 
and in many good and beautiful things, the possession of them 
in peace. What is freedom? Philosophers say, ‘ That thing 
is free which is suspended from naught and from which naught 
is suspended.’ It follows that nothing is free but the first 
cause, the cause of all causes. To lordship it also belongs to 
have these two things: freedom and many good and beautiful 
things, in peace. God is everything good in all and he possesses 
himself in all, for what God is he is in all. When a man says 
he has love and wisdom and will and goodness, he is that, for 
God is that. God-is not naught. God was before naught. 
God has no before and no after: naught has a sequel, its sequel 
is aught. Naught’s fore is God, for he is before naught, and 
naught’s after is aught. God none goes before, none comes 
‘after. Lo, the cause of all things, poised in itself in the in- 
visible light which is himself! God is a light self-poised in 
absolute stillness. That is the one light, the one mode of 
himself that knows and understands itself. The understand- 
ing of the.eternal light that is light from the light, the Person 
of the Son. The Father spoke one word namely his Son. In 
that one Word he spoke all things. The Word of the Father 
is none other than his understanding (conception) of himself. 
The understanding of the Father is none other than the Son, 
and this understanding understands and that which this under- 
standing understands is the same thing that understands it, 
to wit, the light from the light. John says, ‘God spoke one 
Word,’ his eternal understanding (conception) of himself, his 
Son. In this unique conception he conceived all things and 
knew how to make them out of naught, which they are in 

1 See also Greith, pp. 123-4. 

SERMONS 191 

themselves. But their eternal being he was himself, for in 
God there is nothing but God: God is without other. By the 
same token all creatures are the one light since they are im- 
manent in the light. Things all flow out of him as light to 
manifest the hidden light. James (says), ‘Omne datum optimum 
etc” Herein lies proof that all things are light, the Father 
having poured them forth to manifest his concealment, even 
as all things are shown a light to light them back into them- 
selves, those who do not choose to turn away. Ah, those who 
steadfastly remain free from multiplicity, what light, what 
grace is revealed to them! But the Sovran Good is so ordered 
to the soul that she cannot receive it without means, as 
Dionysius says, ‘ The means are light and grace which illumine 
the understanding of the soul.’ What is understanding ? 
Knowing each thing as it is, pure and simple, with unerring 
certainty. Dionysius (says), ‘Ye must free yourselves from 
everything to understand the Highest Good, God namely.’ 
What are we to understand by God ? Power. We must unify 
ourselves so that the one power may energize in us. Also he 
is the one good moving all things towards their good, to himself, 
while he stays unmoved. And he is the one perfect simplicity, 
and the more simple thou art the more wilt thou realize his 
simpleness ; we ought to be perfectly simple, separated out 
from everything and from ourselves as well, our minds laid 
bare before him and the workings of the powers of the soul, 
albeit the chief power, the understanding, is the only one God 
will let work with God. Thus he perfects his own nature in 
her and turns her into himself. She understands with him, 
loves with him, knows with him. We pray, etc. | 

XLV 

Here Meister Eckhart shows how the soul is like Jerusalem and whence - 
comes her holiness and how the divine light shines on five sorts of people. 

Vidi civitatem sanctam Jerusalem (Apoc. 21,). St John saw 
a city and a city stands for two things: safety is one, it cannot 
be harmed ; the other, a concourse of people. This city had 

192 MEISTER ECKHART 

no prayer-house ; God was the temple himself. There was no 
need of light, neither sun nor moon, the glory of our Lord 
illumined it. This city is a symbol of the individual soul. 
‘The soul is the temple of God,’ says St Paul, and St Augustine 
declares, ‘The soul is too strong to be harmed unless she 
consent.’ 

First, as to the peace there should be in the soul. Therefore 
she is called Jerusalem. Dionysius (says), ‘ Divine peace per- 
vades and orders and ends all things; did peace fail to do this 
all would fall into confusion.’—Again, peace makes creatures 
loving and harmless.—-In the third place, it makes creatures 
useful one to another and also beholden to each other. Ye 
cannot have anything for yourself irrespective of others. That 
spells separation. Fourthly, it makes them turn back to their 
original source, to God. 

Secondly, he calls the city holy. According to St Dionysius 
holiness means complete purity, liberation and perfection. 
Purity is sinlessness, and this makes the soul free. Likeness is 
the chief delight and joy there is in heaven; suppose God came 
into the soul and she were not like him, she would suffer 
anguish. (In) St John (it) says, ‘ Whosoever committeth sin 
is the servant of sin.’ Of the angels and the saints we may 
say that they are perfect, but the saints not wholly so, for they 
still feel some affection for their bodies which now in ashes lie. 
In God alone is complete perfection. I marvel that St John 
durst ever say, unless he had seen in the spirit that there are 
three Persons, how the Father empties himself completely 
into the birth, into the Son, and pours himself into the Holy 
Ghost with exuberance of love. 

Holiness, again, connotes withdrawnness from the world. 
God is aught, pure being, and sin is naught and remote from 
God. God made the angels and the soul on the verge of aught, 
that is, not far from God. The soul is created under the 
shadow of the angels, so to say, and indeed they have a common 
nature, but all corporal things are made in the neighbourhood 
of naught, a long way off from God. In lavishing herself upon 

SERMONS 198 

the body the soul acquires merit and must together with the 
body be raised up again to God. When she is free from 
earthly things the soul is holy. Zacheus while he was upon 
the ground could not see our Lord. Augustine (says), ‘If a 
man would be holy let him forsake mundane things.’ It is 
very often said that the soul cannot be pure unless she is 
reduced to her original simplicity, the state God created her 
in; just as from copper gold cannot be made by two or three 
roastings, it must be reduced to its elemental nature. Things 
_ which melt on heating and solidify on cooling are purely of a 
watery nature. They must therefore be turned back again 
to water and get quite rid of their present nature. Thus — 
heaven and science conspire to transmute it into gold. We 
can counterfeit silver with iron and with copper gold: the more 
like the more false, without riddance. It is the same with the 
‘soul. Virtues are easy to talk of, easy to feign, but to have 
them really is extremely rare. 

Thirdly, he calls the city new. New describes what is 
unused or else near to its beginning. Our beginning is God ; 
when we are united with him we shall be new. Some people 
foolishly think that God has for ever been making or keeping 
the things we now see and that he will give these to them in 
time. But divine acts we know to be effortless, as I will show. 
I am standing here and suppose I had been standing here for 
thirty years with my face showing, though nobody had seen 
it, I should have stood here all the same, and suppose a mirror 
handy, if it were held before my face my face would cast an 
image in it without the slightest effort on my part, and if that 
happened yesterday then it would be new and again to-day it 
would be newer still and so on for thirty years or for all time, 
if it was always showing, with perhaps a thousand mirrors, 
and through no effort of my own. God has always in him all 
ideas, not as soul and other creatures but as God. With him 
there is no new or old, but as I was saying of the mirror, with 
us there is old and eternal too. 

When the body is ready God pours in a soul suitable to the 
13 

194 MEISTER ECKHART 

body and having a likeness therewith and because of the 
likeness a liking. Hence there is no one free from self-love : 
they are deceiving themselves, people who think they have no 
love of self. Of course we cannot help loving those things 
which further our progress to God, that is all one with love of 
God. If I intend to cross the sea and want a ship, that is part 
and parcel of the wishing to be over and having gotten to the 
other side I do not want the ship. Plato says, ‘ What God is 
I know not,’ meaning to say, ‘ while the soul is done up in a 
body she cannot see God.’ ‘ But what he is not I.do know 
right well,’ as we note with the sun whose radiance is not to 
be borne undimmed by the air. Dionysius (says), ‘If the 
divine light does shine into me it must be veiled like my soul.’ 
Further he says, ‘ The divine light shines upon five kinds of 
people.’ The first are not alive to it. They are like the 
cattle, too rough to receive it. For instance, if I go upon 
the water and it is rough and muddy, I shall not be able to 
see my face in it because it is so troubled.—The second but 
little light illumines, as it might be the flash of a sword. The 
third receive an impression more like a great flash of light(ning) 
and then dark again. [Meaning all those who lapse from light 
divine and fall into sin.] With the fourth kind this is more 
pronounced, But sometimes he will enter simply by attracting 
her, increasing her desire. If someone is going to fill all our 
laps then let everyone make his lap wide and able to hold a 
great deal. Augustine (says), ‘To receive a great deal a man 
must enlarge his capacity.’—The fifth are aware of a light as 
bright as day but still through a chink as it were. As the soul 
says in the Book of Love, ‘My Beloved looked at me through 
a chink. His face was comely.’ Augustine said, ‘ Lord, some- 
times thou dost give me such great sweetness, if this at its 
perfection is not heaven I know not then what heaven can be.’ 
A master says, ‘ The man who tries to see God, unless he be 
adorned with godly works will be put back to ordinary things.’ 
Is there then no way of seeing God quite clearly? Yes. In 
cantico the soul is telling of it where she says, ‘ My love looked 

SERMONS 195 

at me through the window,’ that is, with nothing between, 
“and I knew it, he was standing by the wall,’ meaning the 
body, which is fallible, ‘and he said, “ Open to me my love,” 
for he is wholly mine in love, he belongs to me and I to him 
- alone, “my dove,” that is, in longing, “my beautiful,” that is, 
in act. “Arise, make haste and come to me. The cold is 
past,” of which everything dies: things all live by warmth; 
“the rain is over and gone,” pleasure, that means, in temporal 
things; “the flowers are coming up in our land”: a foretaste 
of eternal life. ‘‘ Begone, O north wind, which withers up”: 
God is forbidding temptation to hinder the soul any more. 
“Come, wind from the south, blow through my garden and 
make my spices to flow”: God bids all perfections to enter 
my soul.’ 
XLVI 
Beatus vir qui inventus est sine macula (Eccl, 81,). ‘ Blessed 
is the man that is found without blemish and that goes not 
after gold nor puts his trust in perishable riches.’ And in the 
Gospel there is another saying of our Lord, ‘ What shall a 
man give in exchange, or as ransom, for his soul?’ These 
words suggest three noble qualities of soul. The noble conduct 
of the soul includes all that a man is able to do or to give, and 
supposing he loses his soul he will in effect have done nothing 
at all. Then the second noble quality. This he refers to in 
the words, ‘ Blessed is the man.’ And the third noble quality 
of the soul is, putting no trust in perishable riches. A (heathen) 
philosopher says man’s happiness lies in four things. The first 
is a sufficiency of this world’s goods. The man who has succeeded 
in winning wealth and honour is by the denizens of this world 
called a happy man, and this is the basest sort of happiness 
since there is no warranty of its being from God. The second 
happiness is better, it comes of being sound in wind and limb. 
The third kind which is better still is due to a virtuous and 
well-ordered life. The fourth and most perfect happiness of 
all belongs to the man who is full of love and wisdom.—But 
is this true? It is only a heathen philosopher who says so.— 

196 MEISTER ECKHART 

Yes, it is quite true. Then again it is maintained that God does 
not love anyone, share his love with anyone but those who 
dwell with the eternal wisdom, who are housemates with it. 
According to one of the saints all human love is misbegotten 
since it is ever seeking its own selfish ends. If one man seeks 
another it is because he likes him, and if he did not like him he 
would take no notice of him. No one is thinking of his neigh- 
bour but each one of himself. ‘God alone,’ a saint observes, 
‘is altogether kind, for he bestows his favours disinterestedly, 
simply out of goodness, out of kindness, he gives all his gifts.’ 
Suppose a man is forced to seek his own advantage—and some- 
times there is need of this, if he is poor, for instance, and un- 
able to afford to do good for itself—then, says St Augustine, 
‘Seek and find.’ For in everything he does man is seeking 
two things, to get hold of something in which he can rest or 
to get rid of something that is spoiling his rest, supposing he 
has it, and which is a spiritual likeness of eternal rest. He 
says, ‘ Seek and find,’ eternal happiness to wit, in everything 
you do, not ‘ seek it’ in the darkness of this world. Further 
I would say: seek and find, what you seek is all in creatures. 
If the least of the joys experienced by creature came as free, 
as naked and as undisguised as it is implanted and up-springing 
in light divine eternally : if joy did come like this into the soul 
and filled her on a sudden overfull, she would be unhinged, 
it would be insupportable, like sunshine to the eyes except it 
be tempered in the air. No eye could admit the over-brilliant 
light and not be stricken blind. Therefore I say: seek and 
find the likeness of divine light and joy in every creature, but 
not, as you would do with creature to know and take pleasure 
therein. That is the whole cause of sin, man’s turning his 
attention away from God to creature which he enjoys and 
takes pleasure in. 

But if it is a sin in man to be self-seeking then peradventure 
God has that same sin in him, for he is always thinking of him- 
self and seeking to glorify himself. Ile has created all manner 
of things, each one to show forth his glory since no single 

SERMONS 197 

creature could make his greatness known. That is why, the 
Doctor says, he made all sorts of creatures, and the ones who 
showed him best he made in greatest number, angels namely. 
In the overflowing love which he could not contain God spilled 
them . . .! and poured himself out in all creatures when he 
created them and saw them there before his eyes and made 
known his glory therein. I say, and the saints will bear me 

out, that God’s only Son would never have been born but for 
the storm of love and bliss which made God empty himself out 
in eternal love and joy, and that his divine glory was all revealed 
therein and that God loves nothing but himself. He cast forth 
his joy in all creatures in the guise of an angel : in such pleasure 
as man feels in creature he was meant to find this angel of 
divine joy so that by hanging on to him he might be borne into 
God. God is consumed with love for everything. It is infinite, 
eternal, incomprehensible ; he cannot reach the end of it in 
eternity, because it hasno end; he cannot heighten it, for there 
is nothing higher than his love; he cannot comprehend the 
extent of it, for he cannot surpass himself in loving. It is 
fathomless in baptism albeit God cannot bless just as he will 
lest he degrade himself. To love something better than him- 
self would make it higher than he. So God loves nothing but 
himself and creature so far as he finds it in himself and himself 
in creature. What he cast off from himself into creature is 
an image of the love and bliss which in him is infinite. Suppose 
that by grabbing with both hands as quickly as I can with one 
I get a hundred marks with one and with the other nothing 
but a penny. While I can get a hundred marks with the one 
hand only, I should be a fool to lay that idle to get a penny 
with the second. God swallows up everything in his love, for it 
is infinite. And man is behaving like a fool when he looks for 
love and joy in creature, things of little or no value compared 
with the riches of God’s love. The precious part, the treasure of 
the soul is divine wisdom, wherein she is partaker of all divine 
felicity and can gather treasure everywhere, as I have often said. 

1 Dots mark illegible passages in the MS. 

198 MEISTER ECKHART 

We read that (if) a man sacrifices something for which he 
gets nothing in return, he may acquire more merit than if with 
his own hand he offered it upon the altar, for thus he gives 
his gift for love of God and does his will and bears his loss with 
patience so that he makes an offering both of his loss and of 
his will to our Lord God. Supposing my friend dies, one I 
would not part with for a fortune, if I give him willingly to 
our Lord God, though I would not have lost him for all that 
I possess, then I shall have in God a place infinitely larger 
than my friend ever could have filled on earth. He is called 
rich, the man who has a thousand marks of gold and has them 
placed where none can filch them from him. But the riches 
of the soul are safer still, where neither devil nor yet man nor 
even God can get them. God does not want to, but he could 
not an he would. I give God full leave, as I do the lowest of 
mankind, to harm me if he will. Suppose he takes my youth, 
and no thanks to me, I shall not have the sins of it. Suppose 
he takes it with my leave he wrongs me not one whit. Of this 
I am quite sure, nothing but sin can do me any harm. The 
only really noble man is he who turns his back on it, for him 
I feel the most profound respect: whilom unrighteous man 
compact with Holy Spirit gotten to God in true repentance. The 
doctrine is that God loves none but himself and that the soul 
is brought to God by uncreated divine wisdom wherein she 
becomes partaker of God’s bliss, and that God is drawn to the 
soul by love. I am sure that with anyone whose love I have, 
all he has is mine to use and to enjoy. That is the reason why 
God gives his Holy Spirit which more than anything that God 
can give represents his love, that is why, (I say), he gives to 
all his Holy Spirit. St John says in one of his epistles, ‘ God 
is love. He who dwells in love dwells in God and God in him.’ 
According to Boecius the philosopher, ‘ That only is a holy 
life which is able to sum up everything into one perfect and 

unchanging peace.’ May every good befall us, so help you 
and me the ete. 

SERMONS “199 

XLVII 

Domus mea domus orationis vocabitur ete. (Matth. 21,3). ‘My 
house shall be called the house of prayer.’ The visible church 
is called a house of prayer; therein we take the physic of the 
soul in the seven sacraments. And Holy Christendom is called 
the prayer-house of our God; therein our Lord is eaten in his 
sacred body which lives and grows in all its members, in Christian 
people namely. The kingdom of heaven too is called a house 
of prayer; therein rests our Lord in the enjoyment of his 
power, The fourth house of God is the soul; therein our 
Lord is doing his work by means of his grace. 

First of all we want to know who we ought to pray to. Next, 
how we ought to pray. Thirdly, what we ought to pray for. 
And fourthly what is the good of it. 

To begin with let us make it plain that we ought to pray to 
God alone. This was taught by our Lord himself when he 
spoke with the woman at the well. ‘Ye pray ye know not 
where nor unto whom ye pray nor what it is ye pray for; but 
the hour cometh when men shall worship God not only on the 
mountains and in temples, they shall worship him in spirit and 
in truth. For my Father is spirit, and people must pray to 
him in spirit: such true worshippers my heavenly Father 
seeks.’ 

Next, how we ought to pray. Do you know how I know 
or can tell a good man? Not by fasting nor alms nor other 
austerities, but by prayer. He who in prayer can best unite 
himself to God, him I call the best of men, for he is dead to hate, 
to anger and the whole brood of sin. The man who mortifies 
himself in’ these things most will pray the best. A man might 
‘give a kingdom for the love of God and doing nothing further 
. . . he would have done nothing at all. To say but one Ave 
Maria and help to slay the progeny of sin in him would have 
accomplished more. Our Lord says, ‘ Except a corn of wheat 
fall to the ground and die it brings forth no fruit, but if it falls 
to the ground and dies it brings forth fruit abundantly.’ A 

200 MEISTER ECKHART 

man quits his sins and starts bearing fruits; in his sufferings 
he dies and is poured out in grace ;. he challenges faith and 
finds love. All honest work is worship, so the Scriptures say. _ 
And I say so too, provided it is done as worship. Say a man 
fasts and gives alms, if by that means he can reach up to God- 

. . . Thomas says that prayer simply means climbing up to 
God. God says that he is high and man is low; so for a man 
to get to God he must put something under him that will make 
him higher. He has to put under him the things that God 
has made, even the soul herself as to her powers whose work 
can never be perfect in the body. I refer to sight and hearing 
and (the rest of) the five senses, which link up soul and body. 
The soul must withdraw into those powers which can function 
to perfection in the body, i.e. love and knowledge. In these 
twin powers the soul pours out of (and dies to) the world. 
For knowing God I want no eyes (or ears): that needs another, 
spiritual, knowing. To be atoned with God in love I need no 
hands or feet nor any limbs whatever, but stealing forth of all 
created things man locks his spirit up alone with God and 
uniting his spirit with God’s spirit, becomes one spirit with 
God. That is true prayer, and that alone, and only in that 
prayer will God be moved to listen to man’s longings or answer 
his entreaties, for above (time) and place God does his godly 
work alone in me. 

‘Spirit.’ In this wise shall ye pray, not with many words 
nor minding much about the book, but rather where words 
fail in prayer does the prayer get home. When I notice people 
reading with the lips I know they cannot read well. Ifascholar 
is quietly conning a book I can see he is learning. Only for two 
reasons do men pray in words, thus priests and learned men de- 
voted to God’s service sing and read in chorus, it awakens other 
people and spurs them to the interior life. Again, when the 
heart is overwrought feeling grows articulate, finding vent 
in words of fervour and devotion. Had I to counsel anyone 
as to the shortest, safest, sweetest way, the one which God 
most clearly and readily provides, I should say this one, prayer. 

SERMONS 201 

Seven reasons might be given to justify our praying to the 
saints. I will give you only three. The first one is that the 
soul knows the stream of divine light and joy to be over- 
bright and strong at its source and that it floods the highest 
powers of the soul and is then passed on to her lower powers, 
those which are bound up with the body, and afterwards 
proceeds to suffuse the limbs and fill with light the human _ 
body—as the Scriptures say, the body of the righteous shall 
shine forth like the sun at the judgment day. The soul 
is aware that in her superior powers there is a divine light 
and bliss which in her inferior powers is dimmed and as it 
were no light at all compared with the divine splendour, so she 
in her humility durst not pray to God direct but asks the 
saints to offer up her prayer to God, making intercession for 
her. That is good prayer, for by humility God is exalted. 

The second reason is that the stream of divine light and 
sweetness is over-bright and strong for any creature to endure. 
Angels, souls, all creatures, if they were reduced to a single 
angel, to a single soul, could not go back in praise to God. 
Angels are called heavenly birds because they sing God’s praises 
without ceasing, in their exultation rising up to God. Said 
Moses, all waters flow out of the sea and flow back again into 
the sea, and so divine joy is all poured out on creatures and 
flows back with God into God. The soul perceives this clearly, 
but seeing, on the other hand her frailness, she does (not) dare 
importune God for aught, so turning to the saints, to whom 
belongs some power and who have been men, she entreats 
their help. Otherwise it would be impossible that anyone 
should ever want to seek aid from the saints, for God alone is 
altogether kind, and has the widest sympathy ; he feels men’s 
sorrows more than they do themselves, and his pleasure in men’s 
happiness is greater than their own. 

The third reason is that God has destined man for eternity. 
Cne man stands in the presence of another and bows down 
before him, thereby honouring God. Whether he knows it or 
not he really is paying homage to God, and no one who knows 

202 MEISTER ECKHART 

keeps the homage himself but gives it to God. May this prayer 
be perfected in us, so help you and me, etc. 

XLVIII 

Justorum autem anime in manu dei sunt etc. (Sap. 8,). ‘ The 
souls of the just are in the hand of God.’ Why does he say 
‘in the hand’ to describe the nearness of the soul to him? 
For it is rather in the heart or in my soul. . . . By reason of 
three things, (he) says, the (souls) of the just are in the hand 
of God: things are best seen when held in the hand, and God 
can see himself therein and can play therewith. Again, he 
made the soul himself without the help of creature; thirdly, 
the very finest work is done by hand, and with the index finger 
man gauges to a nicety the rough and smooth. There is said 
by one of the Church Fathers (to be) a stream of wisdom in 
this land of exile and the soul knows by whom she has been 
made. They asked him how to get to God. He answered, 
* Know thyself, that is the way to God.’ This saying became 
famous and was written at the entrance to their house of God. 
In this knowledge St Dionysius was accomplished, they called 
him Wisdom and Bird of Heaven. He knew, although he was 
a heathen, all the courses of the stars and of the heavens and 
he read it in the stars when they crucified our Lord. He said, 
* It must be either someone in the world undergoing martyrdom 
or whom nature’s maker is afflicting in some way.’ He saw 
this by the sun having darkened its light and the moon a 
fortnight old was at the full and standing by the sun, which: 
shows that the sun was withholding its beams. As well could 
a man be wise without wisdom or exactly the same first and 
last both at once, so it must be true that our Lord was martyred 
at that hour and St Dionysius’ faith in nature was the beginning 
of his longing for the true faith. Also he has written more 
about the heavenly hierarchies than any saint has done. 

Again, the best work is finished by hand, and God holds the 
soul lovingly in his hand, working upon her, he having got her 
so close to himself that no one can take her away. Our Lord 

SERMONS 203 

himself says, addressing good people, ‘ Who touches you 
touches the light of my eyes.’ Elsewhere he says, ‘ What ye 
do unto the least of mine ye have done it unto me.’ A blow 
upon the head straightway rings through all the limbs. And 
it is the surest sign in man of his union with God that at any 
disrespect to God his heart is wrung with grief as though he 
himself had shown it, regardless of who did, and that his 
heart rejoices at anything redounding to God’s honour as 
though it were his own, and a great deal more so. People’s 
souls are in the hand of God and not their bodies, that is be- 
cause he made the soul without a tool, as it were by hand to 
save her from this exile and be her protector. 

Thirdly, he intends to be her reward himself, that is why he 
brought (her) out of this land of exile into eternal life. 

Three reasons are given why God made the soul himself 
without any instrument or any help from creature. (He) has 
made her, not as a part of his own nature albeit a nature of 
(divine descent. For were she) not of the rank of God it 
would be beneath his dignity to cast (the image of) himself 
into so changeable a creature wherein his godly light might last 
or might go out altogether: that is not fit for God. The chief 
reason why God made the soul himself is the sheer perfection 
of his love; the second is the noble nature of the soul and the 
third the worthlessness of creatures: these three together give 
the explanation. Supposing God had called in any angel to help 
in the making of the soul he must have put into the soul some- 
thing of the angel, for never did an artist paint (a picture), 
carve an image or write the letters of an alphabet but he must 
have copied the pattern in his mind; so neither could a 
creature help God create the soul without in some sort leaving 
its image in the soul. The soul was thus constrained never to 
bear more love towards that creature than towards any other 
and to cease from seeking her own interests and pleasure ; for 
it is by these that the soul becomes debased and adulterated 
with creatures. Further, to constrain the soul to love him 
God has made her after his own image: like his eternal nature 

204 MEISTER ECKHART 

in her (memory) and like his wisdom in her intellect and like 
his goodness in her will. Just as, eternally, he was able in his 
' power to create her and in his wisdom to create her as he would 
and for what happiness he would, to wit his infinite good, so 
has he done in time. When, having learnt the alphabet, I 
write a word upon the page, it is not (myself) I pour out in the 
letters, Iam copying the pattern in my mind. Nor is the soul 
made of the nature of God but in the likeness of the holy 
Trinity, so being tied to God alone, by love to him, she knows 
only peace and comfort. For from likeness springs the greatest 
joy and love and comfort that can be had from virtues or works 
of any kind in heaven or on earth. 

As to the other two, how God tries the soul and how he is 
her reward, that is told elsewhere. May all these things befall 
us, So help you and me the Father, Son, etc. Amen. 

XLIX 

“The kingdom of heaven is like a certain king who made a 
banquet for his son’ (Matth. 22,). In this banquet we are shown 
a symbol of eternal happiness, and happiness is joy and folks 
enjoy themselves chiefly in one way, in feasting namely, 
so eternal joy and bliss are symbolized by feasting. Four 
things contribute towards this enjoyment. The first one is 
discernment, hence a well-assorted company. The second is 
a common purpose; the third is good arrangement: to every- 
one his rightful place; the fourth one, food in plenty, so that 
to each is given what he most desires. In the words of our 
Lord himself, ‘ All things are now ready, bid them to the 
marriage feast.’ God himself is the food. As the Scriptures 
say, ‘Show me thy glory and I shall be satisfied.’ Also the 
place must be large, so that none may come too late, as the 
king’s servants declared, ‘ Lord it is done as thou hast com- 
manded and yet there is room.’ Heaven is so wide that none 
can be too late, there is always room. Not so upon earth. 
Here everyone expects to get more than he deserves. That is 
because we over-estimate our worth. In heaven there is no 

SERMONS 205 

desire for this, it would grieve a man to get more than his 
deserts, since he can see what is due. And he demands from 
no one what is his, for each one reckons his (friends’) joys his 
own in the bond of love. 

Next! let us see what should be their care who are destined to 
enjoy this divine honour. To be beautiful is their first care, 
their second to be noble, to berich the third and wise the fourth ; 
fifthly it behoves them to be strong, and sixth sweet-tempered, 
the seventh to be healthy and live long. All these seven things 
are found in perfection in the bridegroom, our Lord Jesus 
Christ, and his bride the soul must have seven things to 
correspond. So beautiful is our Lord Jesus Christ the sun and 
moon marvel at his beauty. His bride then must (be) pure in 
thought and word and deed. She may not be self-luminous 
but she is virgin-clear, and her clearness is illumined by the 
splendour of our Lord as the sun lights up the deep. Secondly 
our Lord is noble: in heaven he has a Father and no mother 
and on earth he had a mother but no father, so his lineage is 
wonderful, past any man’s conception, as the prophet said, 
‘Who can declare his generation?’ Accordingly his bride 
must be worthy of all honour, in merit ranking higher than 
anything inferior to God, able to praise God as all are praising 
(him in) doing and leaving undone and in suffering, so that 
just as God is seeking to glorify himself in all his works so she 
can seek God’s glory in everything she does.—Thirdly our 
Lord is rich, the Scriptures say that heaven and earth are his 
and he gives life and being to all creatures. So his bride can 
freely confide her cares to him, and it argues gross mistrust for 
anyone who has resigned himself to God to be afraid things 
will go wrong and not to trust in God who is so rich and kind 
to overflowing and readier to give than we are to receive.— 
The fourth thing is his wisdom, this is so lucid it lights up the 
ground of every heart and nothing is hidden from his view. 
Accordingly his bride must mind her ways at all times, every- 
where. If a man a hundred miles away knew all his wife was 
| 1 See also Pfeiffer’s Tractate III (Trans., vol. i, p. 299). 

206 MEISTER ECKHART 

doing she would take good care lest she were put to shame 
before him.—In the fifth place our Lord is strong: from him 
all things come and he has done all work and bettered all our _ 
sins and watched over us, the guardian of our worth. [Salvation 
is with him.] So his bride may hope great things. She must 
be free from strife and fear and weakness. What she cannot 
do he can do perfectly, as the Gospel says, ‘ things to men 
impossible are possible to God.’—Sixth, he is sweet-tempered. 
In the Scriptures he is called a lamb without blemish, having 
not a trace of hate or anger. He is all things to all men, each 
one after his own heart. And his bride must be kindhearted 
and long-suffering, able for his sake to bear (whatever he may 
send her).—Seventh and last, our Lord is immortal whereas 
other things are cursed with the fear of death. So it behoves 
his bride willingly and cheerfully to give up all the things that 
change and pass. Perfection of soul lies in four things. First, 
in never holding anything so dear but she is just as glad to 
part with it as keep it. Next, in never finding any cross God 
sends her so difficult to bear but she would suffer it as lief as 
not on account of the interior reward of his chastisement. 
The third is that God’s justice is as precious as his mercy, to 
herself the same as other people. For many folks approve and 
prize God’s justice towards others but find (it) hard and bitter 
towards themselves, not understanding that God is as perfect 
in his justice as in his compassion. Realizing this the prophet 
‘eries, * Lord, sweeter than honey are thy judgments.’ One 
who does not relish the judgment and justice of our God neither 
will he render thanks for hardship and adversity. He who 
gladly suffers the divine decree has surrendered his own will. 
In the fourth place the perfect soul delights in all God’s gifts, 
in those of other people as much as in her own. That comes of 
perfect love and unselfishness. By these four things the soul 
is transported right out of herself and established in God. So 
help you and me the Father, Son and Holy Ghost. Amen. 

SERMONS 207 

L 

Misericordia domini plena est terra etc. (Ps. 32,). King David 
says, ‘ The earth is full of the mercy of our Lord Jesus Christ.’ 
Whereon St Augustine comments thus, ‘ The earth is full of 
pity since it is full of pain and lamentation, but in heaven 
there is no pity because there is no pain.’ Again, King David 
says, ‘verbo domini celi firmati sunt et spiritu ore eius omnis 
virtus eorum’ (ibid.). ‘The heavens are established by the power 
of the word of our Lord Jesus Christ, of the breath of his mouth 
is the Holy Spirit.’ This passage is well suited to the festival 
of the Holy Trinity, for the words of it suggest the Holy Trinity : 
the power of the Father, where he speaks of the heavens being 
established ; the wisdom of the Son, where he says ‘the Word 
of the Father’; the goodness of the Holy Ghost, where he says 
‘all the power of them by the breath of his mouth.’ St Paul 
realized this when being. caught up into the third heaven he 
saw unspeakable things and cried with a loud voice, ‘ O altitudo 
diviciarum,’ ‘O thou depth of the riches and wisdom and 
knowledge of God, how incomprehensible are thy judgments 
and thy ways past finding out.’ St Augustine’s explanation 
is as follows. ‘St Paul’s being caught up into the third heaven 
merely refers to three kinds of knowledge belonging to the soul. 
The first is knowledge of creatures, which we can perceive with 
the five senses, and all the things which are objective to man- 
kind. Therein we see God but not all of him, only his dense 
body. The second knowledge is more spiritual, we have it of 
the absent, the consciousness, for instance, of a friend a thou- 
sand miles away of whom I have been thinking. I see him in 
imagination, his dress, his form and in time and place. That 
too is nature. In this knowledge he is not intimately known, 
I cannot really know him by means of time or place or colour. 
The third heaven is purely spiritual knowledge, therein the 
soul is rapt away from all objective, bodily things. There we 
hear without means and see without matter, there there is 
(no) red or white or black or blue. In this pure perception the 

208 MEISTER ECKHART 

soul knows God altogether as onefold in his nature and three- 

fold in his Person.’ St John in apocalypsi says, speaking of 
this knowledge, ‘ that light enlightens all who come into this 

world,’ alluding to his then state of gnosis. This clearly means 

that none see God or anything divine save those who have this 

gnosis—who are fully enlightened—not any other kind of 

knowledge. Accordingly he says, ‘all who come into this 

world.’ If he meant this dense material world his words would 

not be true, for here only blindness is rife, he means pure percep- 
tion, his vision of the blessed Trinity, of God as the Word in 

the beginning where the Word is with God and the Word is 

God. St Augustine’s comment is, ‘If he had said more no one 

would have understood it.’ . Furthermore he (David) says, the 

heavens were established by the word of the Lord. And Job 

speaks of the heavens being poured forth at his command. 

These things we know concerning heaven, that it is fixed 
and changeless and pure and all-containing and fruitful. It 
ought to be the same with man who is meant to be a heaven 
for God to dwell in. He should be fixed as the heavens are 
fixed: according to the Scriptures ‘there is no inconstancy in 
the righteous man and friend’s friendship with friend is friend- 
ship with God.’ God has good and evil, joy and grief are all 
one to him. Our Lord says, ‘ The house that is built upon 
a rock will not fall’ The legend is that some two miles or 
more beyond the earth there is no rain or hail or wind, it is 
so still that letters traced in dust or sand stay undisturbed. 
Man who so easily is troubled and disturbed may judge by 
this how far he is removed from God by sin, for it is infinitely 
high from earth to heaven. 

Again, we find purity and clarity in heaven and so we do 
in water. And it is a fact that things held over it give no 
reflection when earth is mingled with it. When it is clear a 
thing held over it will form an image. And the same with 
man: while he is defiled with earthly things he cannot see 
his purity nor yet God’s clarity. But our purity is as it were 
impurity compared to God’s perfection, so the prophet says. 

SERMONS 209 

St Bernard asks, ‘ If the soul is whole in every member why 
does the hand not see the sun, just like the eye? Because the 
hand is not transparent like the eye. If hand or (foot) could 
receive the sun as the eye can do, then hand or foot would see 
the sun as clearly as the eye. Why are sweet things not tasted 
by the ears as well as by the lips or sweet songs and voices 
relished by lips as well as ears? Because they are not fit for 
it. Why does the carnal man not know such spiritual things 
as the spiritual person does?’ The reason, broadly speaking, 
is that anyone who thinks to know and relish spiritual things 
with carnal senses is mightily mistaken. I will not enlarge on 
this [but a heathen philosopher declares that the good man is half 
the time in the toils of sin, that is to say in sleep. In that state 
evil-doers do neither good nor ill. When a good man who has 
something on his mind is visited with pleasant dreams, that is a 
favourable sign. If, dallying with some evil thought the good man 
fights it in his sleep it is a sign that he is on his guard. But why 
does he entertain it? He is asleep, so it does him no harm.] ! 

Thirdly, apprehending heaven and all things he keeps hold 
of them. By love a man is able to retain his hold on every- 
thing, friend and foe alike. Friends he loves in God, foes for 
love of God and he loves all God’s creation for God’s sake 
inasmuch as it belongs to God. 

Fourthly, heaven is fruitful, it helps all kinds of work. 
Heaven does more than the carpenter who builds a house. — 

In the fifth place, heaven is the stool of our Lord. According 
to the Scriptures, ‘Heaven is his stool and the earth his foot- 
stool.’ The heathen philosopher Aristotle says, ‘ Were there 
no house nor place and no materials it would be all one being, 
one matter, which being divided is like another soul.’ As the 
soul says in the Book of Love, ‘Set me as a seal upon thine 
heart.’ God help us all.” Amen.

2 Sievers leaves the sermon unfinished, relegating the last three sen- 
tences to a footnote with the remark, ‘I cannot make this out.’ The 
translation given here is only tentative, but there is obviously a reference 

to Cant. 8,.

210 MEISTER ECKHART 

LI 

Vere dominus iste est in loco isto etc. (Gen. 28,6). ‘Surely God . 
is in this place.’ Jacob said this. ‘If our son takes a wife 
from this country my life will be a burden to me.’ At these 
words he (Isaac) ordered that Jacob be sent to the land of her 
(Rebecca’s) birth. And for two reasons. First, to find a wife, 
and secondly because his brother Esau was his enemy and he 
had to flee from him. 

Egressus Jacob. Having journeyed out of the land of Bersabee 
and toiled till he was weary, he put a stone under his head 
and sank to rest. And in his sleep he saw a ladder standing 
on the earth and reaching up to heaven, with angels ascending 
and descending and God leaning over the ladder and saying to 
him, ‘ Jacob, this land I will give thee for an inheritance and 
I will make thy seed like the dust of the earth and multiply 
it like the stars of heaven spreading thee abroad to the east 
and to the west, to the north and to the south. Cum vigilasset 
Jacob. When he awoke Jacob eried, ‘ Surely the Lord is in 
this place and it is holy, it is wonderful, it is the gate of heaven.’ 
_ This story I interpret in a spiritual sense. 

Body and soul are like twins, they being one man and born 
together, just as the other pair, Jacob and Esau, were born 
both at once, and they are opposed to one another. Esau is 
the trifler, one who occupies himself with frivolous and perish- 
able things. Jacob is the fighter, the victor. Bersabee stands 
for the uncertainty of this present world. On account of these 

"things Jacob fled his country and his brother. Similarly with 
the soul, she is from heaven and body from the earth, from 
father and mother namely, and they are opposed to one another. 
Body wars with soul and soul with body, and according to 
St Paul there is bound to be strife betwixt the soul and body. 
The soul desires eternal, the body temporal things. The soul 
then has to flee from sensual pleasures and all impermanent 
things because, as Basilius hath it, ‘ Perfection means caring 
nothing for things which are not God.’ He gives four examples. 

SERMONS 211 

The first : not caring for mortal things ; next, vain glory, caring 
no more for his own repute than for his neighbour’s. The third 
refers to pleasures of the senses. By these three he is taught 
to reach the fourth: caring nothing for himself. Thus the 
soul finds rest, and sleeping on a stone sees the ladder standing 
with one end on the earth and the other reaching up to heaven ; 
sees the heavens open and God leaning over the ladder with 
the angels going up and down. 

The ladder is an excellent symbol for the soul who in her 
lower powers is resting on the earth—those powers namely 
which are linked to the five senses and give life to the body, 
wherein she is ignoble—and she reaches heaven in her higher 
powers, whereby she knows celestial things. Accordingly one 
master says the soul is a mean ’twixt God and creature, for, 
set at the beginning and at the end of the Most High, she is 
in contact with the lowest. The angels ascending and descend- 
ing are watching over her for she is still (asleep). All the 
works God does below he has first wrought on high. ‘ All form 
and likeness,’ Dionysius says, ‘ he first took and sealed into 
the lowest angels by which means they are brought and sealed 
in creatures; just as an artist, inspired by his art, will carve 
in wood or paint on canvas or the wall, so the angels imprint 
God’s light and consolation on the soul and the soul sees the 
heavens open and God leaning down over the ladder.’ Now 
I tell you for a fact, and it is a strange one known to few, that 
no creature is ever enjoyed by the soul nor does it give her 
any pleasure. (Believe) it or not it really is true. Many a 
man has never seen Rome and yet Rome exists, and in Paris 
there are wonderful things that few people have seen although 
they are there. God was leaning down over the ladder because 
any liking the soul has for creature (she) would not feel unless 
(God) were therein. When she is conscious of this then creatures 
all fall away, and are blotted out from the soul. When Jacob 
awoke out of sleep he exclaimed, ‘ Surely God is in this place 
and I knew it not.’ When he woke up creatures vanished : 
he saw only God. It is the same with the soul. While she is 

212 MEISTER ECKHART 

asleep the angels wait upon her and she needs no help from 
creatures. But when she awakes to the realization of God she 
drives away the angels, and unwilling any longer to bear 
creatures’ ministrations she has recourse to God alone. He 
says we must get at the kernel by piercing the shell. Above 
all we have got to find God, So help you and me the Father, 
Son and Holy Ghost. Amen.
